Chapter Seventy-Eight.
On cutting limbs and spreading bones.
The limbs may decay because of a cause from outside or a cause from inside. If you have treated the corruption with medicinal treatment and it has not been successful, and you see that the corruption is spreading in the member and nothing can stop it.
So you must cut off that member as far as the corruption has spread, so that the patient will be saved from death or a great calamity which is greater than losing the member. And a sign that this condition has set in is when the member turns black until you think that fire has burned it.
Or if it decays after turning black until that decay spreads to what is below that member and takes over the rest of the body. So cut it off quickly, and similarly if the cause of the decay is from a bite by some poisonous creatures such as scorpions or snakes or rodents and similar things.
If the decay or bite is in the tip of the finger, cut off the finger and do not delay the decay so that it spreads to the hand. And similarly if it takes over the hand, cut it off from the wrist joint and do not delay the decay so that it spreads to the arm.
And similarly if it takes over the arm, cut off the arm at the shoulder joint itself. If the decay spreads and you see that it has reached up to the armpit, do not cut off the armpit at all because that would be the death of the patient. And use another treatment as much as the strength allows.
And do the same with the foot, if the decay takes over the toe, cut off the toe at one of the joints. And if it takes over the toes and reaches up to the foot, cut off the leg entirely. And if it spreads up to the knee, cut off the leg at the knee joint.
And if the decay has spread above the knee, then there is no remedy but to leave it and abandon the patient to death. The procedure for cutting off a member or spreading its bones is to tie a ligature at the bottom of the place you want to cut off, and another ligature above the place.
A servant gives the lower bandage down and another servant gives the upper bandage up, then you strip the meat between the two bands with a broad probe until all the flesh is uncovered, then cut it or spread it out. And you should place the linen mesh from all sides.
So that the saw does not touch the correct place and thus excess pain and a hot swelling is exposed. If bleeding occurs during your operation, stop immediately or put some cauterizing thorns on it then go back to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the injured member with a bandage suitable for it and treat it until it heals. And I am telling you an example shown to a man on his leg, this abscess which I have described to you; that is what happened in his leg, blackness accompanied by heat like fire.
And that corruption was the first thing to happen in his finger until he took all of it, so when the man saw the corruption spreading in the member with great pain and heat, he cut it at the joint and it healed.
When a long time had passed, he showed me the same corruption on his hand in another finger of his thumb and I cauterized it with the device that I carried on my arm from medicines after cleansing its body; so the cauterization did not recede and made itself spread in the other finger.
Until the corruption spread in his hand, he summoned me to cut off his hand and I refused him with my plea that I contain the cauterization and I also feared for him death when cutting off his hand because the man's strength was about to fall, so when I failed him, he left and returned to his country.
So I was informed about him that he had cut off his hand completely and it healed, and I only narrated this story to be an aid in what happens from the same type of disease and as a guide that is relied upon and worked with.
Chapter 78.
In the treatment of ulcers and how to inject them with medicines.
If an abscess forms in some of the soft organs and its duration was prolonged until it became full then burst or opened up and all that had been inside came out, and the place remained empty as if it were a vessel.
And the skin over it was destroyed as if by a cloth and its corruption had not reached bone or nerve or tendon, from here it deserves to be called an ulcer and not to be called a gangrene until its duration becomes prolonged until the corruption affects something of these organs.
So when that happens, it is called a gangrene or an erysipela. Its treatment is to cut off all the skin over it as one would remove a cloth, especially if it has been destroyed and become like a rag, and it is certain that it will not adhere back to its place due to its corruption.
If the skin healed and was not fully corrupted, and there is a thick layer of flesh beneath it that can be reached by injection, then look at the ulcer; if it is large and has a foul-smelling discharge, inject into it with Egyptian medicine.
That is Kahl, oil, honey, and ginger, in equal parts mixed together in a vessel and cooked over the fire until the medicine thickens and comes to be like the thickness of honey. Then take from it what you need and mix with water and honey, and inject into the ulcer, tie its mouth closed, and leave the medicine inside for as long as two hours.
Then remove it with a gentle traction, do this for some days until the ulcer is purified and the foulness disappears. And if you cannot obtain this medicine, then inject with ash-water, which is that you take the ashes of grapevine or olive wood, and pour water over them.
Then dress it and wash him thoroughly until you are certain that the abscess has been washed away. If there is no hope of healing with the powder or water of ash, then wash him with water and honey in which a little powdered ginger has been mixed.
Or, if you wish, inject into it honey and drink mixed together because honey has the property of cleansing and washing and the drink is likely to clot the abscess, especially if the drink contains gum that dries up. If you have done this a number of times and are certain that the abscess has been discharged, then inject into it whatever grows in the flesh.
Or take from the ointment a quantity and dissolve it in rose honey and astringent drink, or inject into it some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we have mentioned in the article on ointments. If the mouth of the abscess is narrow so that it does not accommodate the syringe tube.
Then stuff it with a little iron or put into it melted pills mixed in the powdered ointment, or the ointment of sarakun until it dilates. And also if the mouth is wide bring together his cheeks with sutures and leave from him as much as will enter the syringe tube without excess.
And also if the mouth from which the pus flows is raised upwards, then incise it at a lower place so that the pus may flow downwards, because the pus if it becomes congested in the bottom of the abscess prevents the flesh from growing there.
If you cannot incise the abscess in a way that suits you, then make it an open wound so that the pus may flow from it easily, according to what is prepared for you, and do not let anything of the pus become stagnant in it at all.
And as for the medicated plasters which are put on the abscess from outside and how to put them on it, is that you take some of the ointment plastered with what you want and stretch it over a linen cloth and let the cloth be large enough so that the whole abscess may be covered by it. Then it is pricked with a pointed instrument outside the sealed place, making a hole slightly wider than the mouth of the sealed place. Then a soft poultice is spread on another cloth to the size of the hole and placed over it in a different position from the mouth of the sealed place so that the discharge may come out easily therefrom, nor does one remove the large cloth except after.
Days are numerous, and as for that which is on the mouth of the sealed place, it is the one that is removed at all times so that what has collected in it may come out and be known whether the pus flowing from it is abundant or small or purulent or not.
And also pay attention to the place of the sealed itself, whether its owner feels pain in it or does not feel pain there, and is there a swelling in it or is there no swelling; if your treatment has proceeded thus and you see that the matter which comes out on the mouth of the sealed is small.
So know that the sealed place has adhered or approached adhesion, therefore open it on the third day or on the following day; and when you have opened the wound, change the cloth with another and renew the poultice; if after a long time pus of fine fluid comes out from the sealed place do not despair of its healing.
And it will be a near recovery because there are people among whom flesh healing in their wounds is slow, and there are the opposite of them; if after many days pus that is not purulent comes out from the sealed place know that the sealed has not adhered, even if the healing of the sealed is delayed.
And the case is prolonged, so increase in drying medicines and let them be in a state that they are moist in their consistency and dry in their strength, like the soft poultice if it has been increased in it the addition of gallnut; among the most effective treatments is to take the soft poultice.
And it is treated with the sap of rose and he sprinkles on it the old temperate drink in its strength then wraps him up with it and uses it, and perhaps he will do this action if the soft poultice honey has not been found ready if it has been cooked until it becomes bitter and used or taking from the resin and patience and aloeswood and crushing all of them and sprinkling on the honey which is on fire. Then dress it with a cloth and tie it on the repository or cover it with honey that has been cooked until thickened, and lay medicine on it and put the cloth and tie it.
And it is used similarly with long zirawan and asfanjanii salsify and the dill of the cornflower and the separated or crushed and sifted quentinum, and laid on the honey that I described and used; if a long time passes over the repository and he does not recover by this treatment, then know that there is corruption in it.
And it has an effect on the bones or on any of the hard nerves or ligaments, effective treatment by senna as described above.
The 88th Chapter.
In the treatment of gangrene and septic wounds, cutting off an extra finger, and dividing joined fingers.
Gangrene is a flesh that grows under the nail of the thumb or foot and maybe in other fingers, if it becomes old and his treatment is neglected it swells and becomes warm.
or it corrupts and becomes foul until it eats the base of the nail, perhaps all of it is corrupted and maybe the corruption reaches the bone until it has a bad smell and the tip of the finger becomes swollen and its color becomes purple; if you treat him as we have mentioned in the division and do not benefit from treatment.
So you should cut with iron all the growth that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterwards; and that cauterization is very beneficial in this case. And as for if the bone was sound and the nail also sound.
And if the angle containing the nail had compressed the flesh into the inside and made it swollen, then you should put a thin sponge under the angle of the nail which compresses the flesh and lift it up and cut that flesh gently.
If the flesh remains, it is to be treated with potent cauterizing medicines until all of it has been consumed. Then it is to be handled with ointments until it heals. However, if the corruption has affected the bone, it should be cut and removed because the corrupt bone will not heal as long as it remains.
If you see corruption and putrefaction in the finger, cut it off at one of the joints, as previously mentioned. Then treat it until it heals. However, if the nail has been struck or hurt and there is severe pain in it, it should be lanced first.
Then cut the nail with a sharp lancet, slanting from top to bottom. Be careful not to reach the flesh under the nail because you will cause severe pain and an overgrowth of tissue in that area. Then treat the place with what soothes pains.
And as for the supernumerary fingers that occur in some people's hands, perhaps all of it is flesh, and perhaps there is bone in part of it, and perhaps there is a nail in it, and sometimes there is an overgrowth of tissue at the base of one of the joints.
Or the growth may be at some joint of the finger, and it does not move, or it may be at the joint of the finger and perhaps has moved. What is purely flesh should be cut off easily, and what needs to be cut should be cut at its root with a wide lancet.
And as for that which grows at the joint, its treatment is difficult and it should not be cut. But as for that which grows in the finger at one of the joints, it should be cut first, cutting it sharply to the bone, then spreading the bone with one of those saws suitable for that.
Then treat it until it heals. And as for the adhesion that presents itself between fingers, often this is presented and it is what man is born with, or it occurs due to a wound or burn and the like. It should be cut open.
Until the fingers return to their natural state, then insert between them a fragment or an opening saturated with rose oil so that they do not adhere quickly. And separate them or place between them a thin sheet of tin until the area heals as it should.
And also when a union of some fingers by the palm, split that union according to what prepares and corrects its shape.
Section Ninety.
On cutting incisions.
The ulcers are twisted, swollen vessels filled with black pus that occur in most of the body's organs and most frequently in the legs, especially in the varicose veins, pustules and hemorrhoids. It is necessary first to use a strong friction on the black spot several times.
Then remove the blackhead from its owner with a pessary. As for treating them with iron, it will be on two forms: one that cuts and comes out the black blood and the other that makes the pus come out entirely. And cutting is like this: first dip the leg in hot water so that it becomes warm.
Until the thick and black blood is dissolved, then pull the affected leg from above its knee to below its ankle with a compress, then make an incision in one place or two places or three wide cuts.
Then you will allow the black blood to come out with your hand from below the leg to above and from above to below until it comes out enough or as much as the patient can endure, then tie him up and order him to avoid foods that produce black spots and repeat the evacuation and pessary.
When the veins are full and that harms the patient, as for his cure it will be on this form: shaving the patient's leg if he has a lot of hair then immersing his leg in the hamam and dipping his leg in hot water until it turns red and the pus is discharged or with a strong purging.
If he does not have a bathhouse until the member is warmed, then one should cut the skin alongside the vein longitudinally either at its end at the knee or below it at the ankle, and then open the skin with scissors and scrape the vein from all sides so that it becomes apparent to the touch.
And when it appears, he sees that it is red and swollen. If he completes the cutting from the skin, he also sees that it looks like a cord. Then he inserts a probe under it until it rises and comes out of the skin. He then ties it with a blind, smooth scissor, and cuts another cut close to that cut for three finger widths.
Then scrape the skin off from over the vein until it appears, then lift it with a probe as he did and tie it with another scissor as he did first. Then cut another cut or many cuts if that is needed.
Then take it and cut it off at the end of the cut at the ankle, then pull it and take it until it comes out from the second cut, then pull it to the upper cut and do that until you pull it out from all the cuts above them. Then cut it off.
If it does not respond to pulling and taking, then insert a strong thread doubled and tied, and pull it and insert the probe under it and guide your hand in all directions until it comes out. Be careful that it does not break, for if it breaks, it will be difficult for you to take it.
And a bad thing will enter into the abscess from it. If you have taken it all, then put on the place of the incision wool that is soaked in wine or red oil or fat and treat him until he recovers. And if the vein is swollen with folds and wrinkles and twisted to one side.
It has not been straight as we said, so it must be cut at every curvature of its folds and places of alignment then tied with ligatures until you have completed all of them. Be careful when cutting into it that you do not cut the hair or injure it for this will cause you difficulty in pulling it out and save your effort.
Picture of the caterpillar which is used to split open the ligament.
Image of the blind saw.
It should not be twisted like the other ligatures and its tip should not be sharp so as to cut the hair or injure it. And it should have a thick and smooth bend, for if it were thin it would cut the hair with its edge; but it should have a blunt thickness as we said.
Chapter Ninety-One.
In the extraction of the ligament.
This ligament is formed in the legs of hot countries, such as Hijaz and Arab lands, and also in hot but little fertile regions. It may be formed in other places of the body besides the legs, and it is formed because of an inflammation that occurs beneath the skin.
As it also occurs inside the living bodies and the worms, the leech and the creeping worm, and the skinworm which is formed between the skin and the flesh. The sign of the beginning of this ligament's formation that there will be a severe inflammation in the leg, then the place will swell, then the ligament will start to come out from that swollen place.
As if it were the root of a plant or an animal, so when its end appears, it should be wound on a small piece of lead, which is worth from one to two dirhams, and tied, then leave the lead hanging from the leg.
So, whenever something comes out of it towards the outside, wind it around the lead and tie it. And if it prolongs a lot, cut some of it and wrap the rest, but do not cut it from its root before all of it has come out, for if you cut it, it will contract and enter into the flesh, causing a bad abscess in that place and a poor ulcer.
Therefore, it should be treated and a little drained until it comes out of the last part of it and nothing remains in the body; sometimes this discharge can come out of this fluid in some people to its length five fingers and ten.
And I was informed that it came out for a man twenty fingerbreadths long; if the discharge stops in your case while being treated, then insert a probe into its hole and push it very deeply with the body until all the material inside is drained from it, and try to clean the area with medicines for some days.
Then treat it with a remedy for tumors. This discharge can be very numerous, especially if it appears in the joint of the foot or in the foot itself; it will cause many openings to appear and each opening will drain a branch of fluid, as we mentioned earlier and what has been previously discussed.
The second chapter ninety.
In the incision on the worm that develops under the skin and is called the plague of cattle.
This disease is called the plague of cattle in some countries among us, due to the fact that cattle are often exposed to it; it is a small worm which develops between the skin and flesh and spreads throughout the body, rising and falling.
It is palpable to the touch when it migrates from one organ to another until it bursts where ever it bursts in the skin, coming out and being formed of a mixture of some viscera as well as being exposed to tapeworms and living creatures and love of locusts in the abdomen.
And it is expected from its harm that if it migrates in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open it up and come out, thereby destroying the eye; this often happens. If you want to treat it and take it out, then this can only happen when it has migrated and appeared for the sense of touch.
You should tie a string tightly over and under it, then cut through to it and take it out. If it has sunk into the flesh and you cannot find it, carry the place towards the fire until it burns them. The worst that is expected of it is to spoil the eye as we said.
If you see it has sunk in the head near the eye, tie a string tightly under it on the forehead with a good pull then cut through to it and take it out. It is necessary for the surgeon to cleanse his body with simple medicines for foul and bad humors and to abstain from nourishing foods that bring about inflammation.
Chapter Ninety-Three.
In the incision on the disease known as the Naffer.
This disease that is called in our land Naffer is a pain that manifests itself in some organs and then transfers from organ to organ, and I have seen it as I described it to you.
I was summoned to an elderly woman in some villages, I made an incision on her arm and inflated it with a slight puff of air from the sinew vein. When an hour had passed I saw that inflation spreading upwards like a maggot climbing up with the ligature towards her shoulder as quickly as mercury.
If it moved from place to place, the pain would leave that place and settle in the shoulder. Then I sat with her for an hour and it spread throughout the body until it reached the other arm. She then told me that her whole body was rotating as I had seen.
I was amazed by the speed of its transfer from organ to organ and I had not seen this disease with my own eyes in such a manner before, except that I have seen groups of people whose pain transfers from one organ to another but I did not see it like I saw it in this woman.
I did not attribute that to anything, except that the woman was from the Bedouins, with a rough body and uncovered veins, so it became clear for the sense that there was this wind-like movement and it should not be apparent in people of comfort and well-nourished bodies with hidden and veiled veins.
If you wanted to cure him and he felt pain, then on top of it and underneath it with the forceps; cut into it until that congested pus comes out and clean the place.
If you did not see it with your eye, then treat him by fomentation of the body and that which disperses the pus and expels it such as camphor and musk and like them among medicines.
The fourth part and ninety.
On removing arrows.
The arrows may differ in accordance with their types and the parts of the body where they are located. As for their differences in accordance with their types, some are large and small, among them are those whose glass is inflated and those whose glass is solidified, among them are those that have three angles and four angles.
And among them are those that have tongues and among them are those that have shreds. And as for the ones that occur in accordance with the organs they are located in, they are of two kinds: either the organs are major inflated organs like the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, stomach, bladder, etc. So when an arrow falls into one of these organs and signs of death appear in it, as I have described to you previously, then you should avoid removing that arrow from it, because death will befall its owner in most cases. And when these bad signs did not appear to you and the arrow was not hidden in the depth of the organ, then take it out and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain if an arrow hits it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane on the brain, then it presents from that a very severe headache, madness and dizziness.
And when these bad signs did not appear to you and the arrow was not hidden in the depth of the organ, then take it out and treat the wound. And among the signs of the brain if an arrow hits it and penetrates the bone and wounds the membrane on the brain, then it presents from that a very severe headache, madness and dizziness. And redness in the eyes and redness of the tongue and spasms and confusion of mind and a single utterance. And perhaps blood comes out from the nostrils or ears, and perhaps speech is cut off and the voice is gone, and fluid white like gruel comes out from the place of the wound, and it flows like meat juice.
If these signs appear to you, then refrain from treating the patient and removing the arrow if it has not been removed. As for the signs of the arrowhead, when the heart is in a situation where it appears close to the left breast and feels as though it has been lodged in something solid rather than something empty.
Perhaps the arrow had a movement like that of the pulse and there was bleeding from the wound with black blood, followed by coldness of the limbs and a chill, and fainting; know then that death is inevitable. The sign of the arrow if it has pierced the lung is the discharge of frothy blood from the wound.
And the vessels which are around the neck will swell, and the colour of the patient will change, and he will breathe deeply and request to inhale cold air. If the arrow has reached the diaphragm that is in the chest, then it will be near the small ribs and there will be great breathing with severe pain.
And he will stiffen and all the members of the neck will move, and if the arrow has reached the liver then severe pain will follow and blood will come out of the wound which resembles the colour of the liver. And if the arrow has reached the stomach then perhaps something not yet digested will come out from the wound.
It is clear, and if the arrow has reached the abdomen and pierced it and something from the feces or stool or urine came out of the wound, then there is no hope for his treatment nor for removing the arrow. And if the arrow has reached the bladder and urine came out.
And something protrudes from it to the outside and the pain on the patient intensified, know then that he is doomed. And as for the other organs such as the face, eye, throat, shoulder, elbow, lower back, thigh, leg and similar organs, take charge of treating them most of the time.
If the arrow did not hit a main vein or tendon and was not poisoned, and I inform you of some of what I have seen regarding these arrows so that you may deduce from it your treatment, namely that an arrow had pierced a man's nose in its root.
I extracted it from the other side under the prominence of his ear and he was well, without anything bad happening to his eye. And I extracted another arrow from a Jew who had shot one in the corner of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had disappeared except for its small tip which stuck to the splint.
And it was a large arrow made of assembled iron, square in shape, smooth and without a head. The Jew was well, and nothing bad happened to his eye. And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian, which was an Arab arrow.
And it was the one who had two eyes, so I slit open his throat between the necks and it had sunk into his gullet. So I manipulated it until I extracted it and the Christian recovered. And I extracted another arrow from a man whom it had pierced in his stomach, and we had estimated that he would die from it.
So when the period of about thirty days had passed and nothing in his condition had changed, I cut into the arrow and cleaned it off and extracted it. And he recovered without anything bad happening to him. And I saw a man whom an arrow had pierced in his back so I sealed over the wound on him.
So when seven years had passed, the arrow came out from its tip and exited. And I saw a woman whom an arrow had pierced in her stomach so I sealed over the wound and it stayed without anything changing in her condition or causing her any harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man whom an arrow had pierced in his face and I sealed over the wound and he remained without feeling much pain. And like this was many a case. And I extracted an arrow from a man of the Sultan's cavalry who it had pierced in the middle of his nose, which had slightly leaned to the right side.
And the arrow was completely gone, so I was called to treat him after the arrow had been in for three days. And I found that the wound of the arrow was very narrow, so I searched it with a thin probe and did not feel anything in it. But he felt pain and discomfort under his ear from the right incision.
I was relieved that this arrowhead did not break off, so I applied a strong absorbent to the affected area, and then stroked it with my fingers, hoping that swelling would occur in the area and a sign of the arrow's presence would appear. So I cut into him and there was no incident indicating that the arrow had reached the site.
I applied absorbent to him for many days, but no incident occurred. So I closed the wound and the patient remained numb from its removal for a period of time until one day he felt the arrow inside his nose. I was informed of this and put on the wound a strong, acrid medicine for many days.
Until I opened and explored him, and felt the tip of the thin arrow which was stuck in the bone. Then I continued to open the wound with that strong medicine until the end of the arrow became apparent to us. And so it remained for a period of time, about four months.
Then, when the wound had expanded and I could insert instruments into it, I grasped it with forceps and moved it. But he did not respond by exiting on his own. So I continued to apply various types of forceps to him until one day I was able to grasp him firmly with forceps such as those shown in the picture at the end of this chapter.
And the physicians would say that the cartilage of his nose was incurable, so I cured it and closed the wound, and the patient was completely healed without any harm. And I am informing you of how I removed some arrows, so that you can use this as a guideline or example for what I have not mentioned.
Because the details of this art and its subtleties cannot be grasped through description or contained in a book. Rather, the ingenious practitioner measures little for much, fills in what is missing, and deduces a new action and a new device at unexpected emergencies.
If she is removed from this craft, I say that the arrows go out of the organs in which they were inserted into two types: either by pulling from the place from which they entered or from the opposite side, and those that come out from where they entered.
Either the arrow is prominent in a place of flesh, it pulls and comes out; but if you do not manage to pull it out at its due time when it has occurred, then you should leave it for some days until the flesh surrounding it softens, so that it may be easy to pull out and take out, and similarly if it had been inserted in a bone.
And if you were not able to take it out, leave it also for some days and repeat pulling and moving it every day; it will come out. And if you were not able to take it out after a few days, then it is necessary that you perforate around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine awl until it expands for the arrow, then pull it and bring it out.
And if the inserted arrow is in the skull bone and has penetrated into one of the brain cavities, and some of those signs that I have mentioned to you have appeared from the wound, then stop pulling the arrow and leave it until its condition settles after a few days.
Then occupy yourself with pulling the arrow and taking it out; if it has been inserted deeply and you were not able to pull it, then use a curette around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the place until it heals, and as for if the arrow had disappeared in a part of the body. And it has disappeared from the sensation, then probe with a spatula; if you felt it, then pull it out with some tools that are suitable for pulling it out; and if you were not able on it because of the narrowness of the wound and the far distance of the arrow in the body cavity and there was no bone, nerve or vein.
Split it open until the wound expands and you can extract it with a needle. If he has ears, hold onto them, clean out any flesh that is lodged in them from all sides using every trick at your disposal; if you cannot clear the flesh from the broken ears, break them further and spread them apart so as to be rid of it.
And if you try to remove the arrow in any part of the body, then use a feathered spatula in all directions until you can extract it and be gentle so that the arrow does not break off and is difficult for you to pull out and remove. If you cannot do it with your time.
Leave it for a few days until the flesh around it rots, then try again; it will be easier that way. If bleeding occurs, treat it with what we mentioned in its place, and conserve your effort from cutting veins or nerves or tendons, and use cunning.
With any face, you can extract the arrow with gentleness and caution and stability as I have described to you. And it is your duty when pulling out the arrow that the awl be on the shape that the arrow was in at the time of its fall, for this will be more suitable.
If you cannot do that, then use what forms are possible. And as for the arrow which comes out from the opposite side, either something has protruded from it to the outside or else you will find the tip of the arrow by touch on top of the skin close by and see it emerging.
Split it open and make the incision as deep as a feathered spatula can go, then pull it out; it will be easier for it to come out. If it becomes stuck in bone, extend your hand on a turn so that the arrow is affected by the bone and widens for itself, then pull it out.
And otherwise, leave it for a few days and then try again until it comes out. If the barb of the arrow is in it, push it with it. And if the barb has fallen and you wanted to use pushing, insert into it the prepared instrument so that its groove enters the hollow of the shaft then push it with it.
If the arrowhead is loose, push it with a tool that enters into this cavity; the arrow will come out easily. If the arrowhead is poisoned, you should cauterize the flesh in which all the poison has accumulated if possible, then treat him as is suitable for that.
If the arrow is in the chest or belly or bladder or side and you can cut into it with a knife, cut into it and be careful not to cut any artery or nerve and remove it.
Then sew the wound if necessary, then treat him until he recovers, A picture of a calabash which attracts arrows.
Its edges would be like a bird's beak, it has been made like an awl if you grasp the arrow or something you didn't leave, and different sizes are made from them, big and small and medium-sized according to the size of the arrow and its thickness and the wound's width and narrowness.

The fifth and ninetieth chapter.
In letting out veins.
The veins which are commonly let out in the body, thirty veins, of them sixteen in the head, the two jugulars behind the ears known as the Hisseens and the two temporal arteries on the forehead known as the Zahrans and the two veins at the corners of the eyes known as the Nazarenes.
And the vein standing in the middle of the forehead and the vein at the tip of the nose and the two carotids in the neck and the two veins in the upper lip and the two veins in the lower lip, these veins are known as the Jiharik. And the two veins beneath the tongue.
As for the veins which are cut in the arm and hand, there are five veins. One of them is Khaifal, which is on the posterior side and the common people call it the vein of the head. And Alkhal, which is the middle one, compounded from a branch of Balsiq and a branch of Khaifal, and the common people call it the vein of the body.
And the Balsiq, which is located on the anterior side and is also called the axillary vein. And he is also called the belly vein by the common people. And the arm tendon, which is located on the crest and adheres to it and appears distinctly above the wrist, with Elsilam.
And this is the vein which is between the two shins, it has two branches. And in the leg and foot there are three veins. One of them is under the knee from the posterior side. The second one is the saphenous vein and its location is at the ankle from the anterior side. And the vein of the heel, its location is at the heel from the posterior side.
And in the other leg there are three veins similar to it. As for the two veins behind the ears, their benefit is cutting them for chronic and acute swellings and eczema and chronic bad sores of the head. How to cut them I have described.
And you remove the head of the vein and rub its back at the place of the two veins with a coarse cloth, very well, then you strangle the vein around its neck with an awning until the two veins appear and their places behind the ears in the low places on the head are similar to them with your finger, where you feel its pulse under your finger there you make it with a needle.
Then you take a thin scalpel which is known as Nashal, and you insert it under the vein in the skin until the blade reaches the bone. Then you lift your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with the skin in a separated cut, and the length of the cuts is about two clenched fingers.
In this way you send the power that you want from the blood, then tie them with bandages and leave them until they heal. The two arteries in the forehead are useful for chronic fever, severe headache and constant inflammation which proceeds to the eyes.
And how you remove them as I have described to you, tie the neck with a turban until the sweat glands appear clearly and their pulse is evident under your finger. Then learn by needle and raise the skin from the point of sweating to above it with your left thumb.
Then insert the lancet from below and raise the sweat upwards, cut it as you did with the other two and send from the blood to your need then release the turban's grip and put your finger on the sweat for a moment.
Then put a thread and bandage on it and tie from above tightly and leave it until it heals, its mention and cutting and bleeding are mentioned in the beginning of the book. And as for letting out the sweat of the forehead, its usefulness is after having let out the venous blood for chronic facial diseases such as eczema, ulcers and fine redness.
And how you remove it as I have described to you, tie the neck with a turban until the sweat appears, then take the instrument which is called the saw and this is its shape:.
You place the pointed part of the saw at the same sweat and strike from above with a comb or something else in its direction, and let the blood flow into the container that you want, then release the turban's grip and tie it tightly until it heals.
And it may be opened with a broad lancet, but it is not suitable that the lancet has a sharp tip like all lances, rather it should have a slightly wide tip and make an incision on the fractured part because the bone is near, so maybe the thin lance would break in it.
As for the two leeches in the eye socket, their benefit is against the ailments of the eye from jaundice, redness and inflammation, as well as face diseases. As for how to extract them, it is that one tightens the handle's neck with a head cloth then extracts them while you stand on his head and let the extraction be slightly long with a small broad pipette slightly.
If the place is not fatty, then if the pipette has a thin end it may break, and your need will be sent by the blood, you put on them a cotton thread and tie him up for one night then remove them. And as for bleeding from the nose it is beneficial from acute fever.
The severe headache and the diseases of the face such as the red flush which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic. And how to extract it: one tightens the handle's neck then takes hold of his nose with your left hand and takes a thin long pipette.
You insert the pipette in the middle of his nose itself while holding his nose straight because the blood does not appear to feel there then the blood will come out from its vein, and you should hold your hand with the pipette a little bit and send from the blood what you need then tie him up for a night because it dries quickly.
And as for the warts, the benefit of extracting them is against shortness of breath and beginning of leprosy and black diseases that appear on the surface of the skin like black scabies and pustules and bad ulcers. And how to extract them: one tightens the handle under them in his neck with a band then the maker stands on the head of the handle.
And the operator sits on a chair then bleeds the leech to length with a wide and slightly deep cut then takes out the moderate amount of blood or according to what he sees as his need to that then does the same with the other leech then removes the band.
The arteries are ligatured moderately so that they do not strangulate the wound, and are left until tomorrow; for it will heal. As for the veins of the neck, a benefit: their ligation after the evacuation of the purulent fluid is beneficial in case of ulcerations in the mouth, corruption of the gums, bad ulcers, and splitting of the lips, and bad ulcers.
The ones that are in the nose and around it, and how to treat them: The patient sits in front of you and binds his neck with a turban then turns his lips and looks at the veins which he sees one on the right side of his lip and the other on the left side and you can tell from them by their darkness.
And that is also because their surroundings are dark purple veins, so cut them off with disconnected cuts. If it is difficult for you and you cannot determine what they are, go ahead to cut the largest and most apparent one, and do likewise with the veins in the lower lip.
And as for the two veins under the tongue, a benefit: their ligation after the evacuation of the purulent fluid for the ulcers that occur in the throat due to consumption and diseases of the mouth. And how to treat them: the patient sits between your hands with his head facing the sun, raises his tongue and looks at it from one side a vein and from the other side a vein, their color is towards blackness.
Then ligate them and take care not to meditate in cutting them because under them are nerves, so perhaps there will be an appearance of bleeding from those nerves. And as for the three veins that are ligated in the armpit, they are usually treated by their ligation in people generally, and their ligation is on two faces.
Either a prick with a wide thin reed or an olive needle into the skin, or a cut with a sharp lancet and it is the awl [and this is its shape]. The picture of the fine narrow awl.
It should be broad as you see to suit the opening of swollen and prominent vessels that appear on the surface which contain thick, dark blood. This is the image of the olive oil lancet.
And this lancet is narrower and finer, suitable for opening vessels containing thin, yellowish blood. This is the image of the thistle lancet.
This thistle, which is suitable for cutting, comes in types of broad and narrow according to the size and width of the vessels, and it can be used instead of others. It is well-known among craftsmen.
And as for the Basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, its benefit in being cut is that it draws blood from the ulcers that form under the neck and throat, following the chest and abdomen. It should be warned to the person cutting it and there should be a ligature on top of it, lest they err and increase in piercing with the lancet, which would sever the ligament beneath.
A hemorrhage will occur, so it should not be cut with a piercing lancet but rather cut with the thistle, and if the Basilic vein does not become clearly visible, it is advisable to take notice of this and adjust to something else or seek some of its branches.
Or cut its place with the arm ligament as it is clear and cut it with the thistle lancet as we said, and if you want to cut it directly then before tightening the arm, palpate the location until you identify the site of the pulse then mark it with a stick.
Then bind the arm and cut the vein with a deep incision by the thistle lancet as we said, and search so that the blow falls far from the site of the ligament, and when you see an inflation in the place where you marked with the stick at the time of tightening the bandage, then that inflation is the swelling of the ligament, avoid it.
If you see the blood when aspirating to come out like urine of a child, and it is thin red, then know that it is from arterial blood. So hasten. Place your finger on it for a long time then withdraw your finger. If the bleeding stops, then tighten the arm.
Leave him and warn the physician not to neglect him, let it be on the neck and do not move it for some days until he recovers. If the bleeding does not stop and you are forced in your presence to administer medicine, then ligate the artery if it appears to you. For its ends will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a sheet of thick linen, and split it in half, then bind the one half tightly to the place of the wound with ligatures and bandages until another day. If the bleeding stops; otherwise treat him as mentioned above with the application of compresses cutting off the haemorrhage, and cut his vein which is not difficult in most cases.
For the place of a small wound and where the ligature is in the arm inform him. And as for the black vein, it is beneficial to prevent its attraction drawing blood from above the head and below the body to the place because it is composed of a branch of the venous sinus and a branch of the great vessel as we have said.
It is necessary for the aspirator to be on the neck of the vein, because under it there are nerves. If he increases the punctures of the syringe and hits the nerve bundle, pain will result which will be difficult to see and perhaps not recover at all. And this nerve bundle is often felt.
If it is subtle in some people and thin so that it cannot be seen, then make your aspirating needle cut it with the lancet and avoid the nerve bundle as much as possible. If the vein is between two nerves, then cut the vein longitudinally. And as for the great vessel, it is beneficial to prevent its attraction drawing blood from the head.
What is beneficial for the eye and should be used in this ointment, if you wish to separate it; to separate it by means of a needle made from olive oil or a broad needle made from rosemary because it cleans all the vessels since there is no artery nor nerve beneath.
Unless that it should be your duty to avoid with the needle the head of the muscle only and to seek the soft place, and it will not harm him if he is not struck by the first blow, so return on him with a puncture except that perhaps swells in some people if not punctured in the first blow.
But that swelling will not harm him anything, and as for the manner of puncturing and its complications and what should be done in its treatment first, it should be known that puncturing is either to be used only in the preservation of health.
And it is either to be used in diseases, and that which is used in the preservation of health and its maintenance and prevention of occurrence of diseases that puncturing should be done in one of the three veins located in the armpit, namely al-qifal, al-akhal, or al-basliq, and puncturing should be done at the beginning of spring if there are signs of fullness.
And puncturing should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after the day has passed for three hours, and as for puncturing which is used in diseases it does not have a limited time but whenever necessity and need calls for it by night or day.
And in every hour and at any time, and it should not be that boys are punctured until they reach fourteen years, and the elderly who have passed sixty years should not be punctured, but if one is determined to puncture for any reason.
He should be cleaned with a cup before puncturing with a fresh needle if there is much turbidity in it, so that dirt does not attract to the veins when puncturing from the cup and cause harm to the main organs. And a drunkard or an epileptic or an intoxicated person should not be punctured until that state has passed over them.
Caution must also be exercised in case of a rupture of the uterus, its discharge, its afterbirth and an excessive frequency of coitus, as well as in all other physical or mental exertions that exhaust one's strength. Then examine the relaxation of the humors beforehand if the blood is thick.
With food, drink and medicines if it is possible for him to do so; then he should enter the bath if no obstacle prevents him or some physical exercise agrees with him so that the blood may be thinned and its bleeding take place in the morning as we have said, and so that his chest be cleared of all bad psychological states.
Like worries, anger and fear and all the bodily obstructions such as excessive fatigue, too much exertion, coitus and things similar to these. He prepares his surroundings with things that are usual among people in their use of fragrances, scents and amusements and similar things; every person according to what he can.
Then the surgeon sits on a cushion higher than that on which the dissected one is seated. He extends his arm and the surgeon compresses it with his hand twice or thrice, then tightens the ligature and twists it twice or thrice, the tightening being moderate.
Because the tightening should not be excessive lest it prevent the flow of blood, or on the other hand that it relax to such a degree as also to prevent the flow of blood. Then after the tightening the dissected one should be compressed with both his hands together, with the palms touching each other.
Until the veins swell and become perceptible to touch. Then the surgeon compresses gently with a little oil the dissected one, particularly; then he places his left index finger on the vein under the place he wants to cut a little so that the vein may not flee from the stroke. Because among the veins there are some like a cord that flee when cut, and others that are full of air, so that when you place the knife on them they sink below the knife and deceive the surgeon, and do not open the vein; or if it does open then it is only narrow.
Therefore, the surgeon should firmly grasp and be cautious in these matters, then proceed with the lancet. If he is able to puncture the vein at that spot, if not he should repeat the process once again under or above that place with a probe, unless the location becomes inflamed.
If the spot becomes inflamed or suppurates, leave it for a day or two and do not tighten the bandage because perhaps there may have entered a hot pus; then let him return to puncture if he wishes. If the lancet pierced and the opening was small and the bleeding was thin.
And he feared that the amount of blood he wanted would not come out, so he reinserted the lancet into the same puncture itself, holding it straight and increasing the opening a little, and do this with the probe before the spot becomes inflamed, because in many people the spot will become inflamed at the small puncture.
If he sees that it has become inflamed, do not add to it at all because it does not benefit you anything; put some olive oil on it instead, for it makes the bleeding easier and it is better in this place than oil itself or any other ointment.
Use olive oil ointment for all your venesections when it is difficult to get the blood flowing, and that the Trierac of Faruq and Shakhsiniya may also be done by applying one of them on the place, then the blood will flow and dissolve if it was thick.
If a swelling occurs at the site of the venesection and it is large, which often happens, especially to those who have only had one venesection or if the vein was punctured small, hasten and put on the swelling a sponge soaked in warm water and salt, then hold it for an hour, then it will dissolve.
And it is advisable to do this after the blood has completely come out of the vein itself or another one, but if something black or green remains in the place after some days then it does not harm; if you wish, apply on it something of dissolved patience and mercury.
If you find a fluid or pus from the fumitory or similar, and often a swelling and inflammation occur when aspirating the phlegm, put your hand on it; if you find it, it will become indurated when you press on it; then that is a severe inflammation. Be careful not to place anything of what we mentioned on it.
If it happens that arterial blood is flowing from it, tie him up with what is in it so as to compress the place and then treat him with the rest of the treatments until he recovers; and it should be bled for each person according to his strength and the prevailing color of the blood.
If the blood is black, let it be bled until it becomes red; and likewise if you see it thick, let him be bled until it thins; and likewise if it is hot, let it go until its heat subsides. It should be for someone who is full, strong and in need of bleeding a large amount at once.
It should be made so that the letting of sweat is wide, and for someone who is weak it is contrary to this; it should be let out in episodes and the puncture should be narrow. The best thing used for letting of sweat is a curved, angled and split instrument, not a pointed one.
And this type of bloodletting is safe from bleeding and cutting a nerve, and it is better and more secure than bloodletting by venipuncture. And if he is accustomed to bloodletting through the forehead, then he should be given something to eat before the bloodletting, such as soaked bread in rosewater or saponaria.
If he is melancholic and bloodletting is done for him in three or four sessions, if his temperament has been calmed then let him take before the bloodletting a bread soaked in sweet wine or in honey water scented with musk or in rosewater.
If a headache occurs due to bloodletting and its cause was the abundant exit of blood, then let him be given meat water and delicate rosewater drink, and let him be perfumed with galena and his chest be fumigated with it; and he should use what we mentioned in the treatment of headache.
That which is from venesection, and as for him who wants to relieve his arm and shed its blood again it should be for him whose impediment was from a great venesection and his power weak that the blood be shed little by little with the measure of power in successive days.
And as for him who wants to relieve his arm and shed its blood again, and whose body was strong, he should do it on the seventh or eighth hour from the first venesection. And as for him who wanted to attract his blood from his body to the opposite side of where it fell, he should return to it in the second or third day.
And as for him who had a lot of blood in his body, it was heated and intensified and he developed fever, it should be taken out from him in one blow, and the great amount taken out, and the incision widened until the fat is exposed after all the conditions of venesection have been met. And you should put your hand on his pulse when the blood is flowing in order that death does not occur instead of the fat. And it often occurs if the operator was ignorant and sleep occurred, and it should not be done if you want to relieve the arm and shed its blood again.
And if the orifice of the sweat had closed and it became difficult to exit the blood, you should compress him strongly or twist forcefully. For that relieves a lot, but perhaps you should leave him until he becomes separated again, or move with the blade of the lancet what has coagulated of the blood in the orifice of the sweat.
Or lay something of salt on him that had dissolved in water, or lay on him some of the galenic or shakhzaniyatu purgative and compress him with a gentle compression until the blood comes out. If the sweat has become inflamed then leave it alone.
And do not touch it until the swelling subsides. If necessity demands shedding the blood again, and there is no choice, then either release it above that place or release it in the other arm or in the other sweat.
And as for venesection of the arm vein, it is done in place of the lacrimal duct and the branch of the saphenous vein if they are not found or are hidden. The manner of doing this is to have the operator put his hand into hot water until the ligature reddens and the veins become apparent. Then pull it over with a band a little tightly, at an intermediate tightness.
Then make the incision of the vein at a slight bending, neither in length nor width. Let the opening be wide and your incision for it be above the wrist joint a little. If difficulty occurs with the blood to come out, replace the hand in the hot water vessel and let the blood flow into the water.
Until your need is fulfilled. If you are in the summer days, you may dispense with putting the hand back into the hot water, but more often it will make the blood flow into the hot water in winter time. The venesection of this vein is more tranquil than all veins. Because there is no artery or vein under it. And as for venesection of the axillary vein from the right hand, it is useful in the ailments of the liver and the manner of doing this is to hold the upper arm with a band or your hand after you have put it into hot water until the vein becomes swollen.
Because there is no artery or vein under it. And as for venesection of the axillary vein from the right hand, it is useful in the ailments of the liver and the manner of doing this is to hold the upper arm with a band or your hand after you have put it into hot water until the vein becomes swollen. It becomes very clear to the touch then make an incision on it a little bent and if you cut through it entirely, that will not harm him at all. Be careful not to insert your hand with the lancet deeply because there is the nerve of the fingers under it and the place is exposed from the flesh. Then put the hand back into the hot water.
And let it flow with the blood in it. For if you do not put it back into hot water, the blood will clot in the vein and refuse to flow. If you have taken out the amount of blood needed, place a mixture of oil and salt on the vein so that it does not clot quickly.
And also do the same with every narrow limb, and its benefit is to remove it from the left hand for it is useful for ailments of the spleen. And you do the same in removing it as I have done in the second one. But the removal of the saphenous vein is beneficial for diseases that are in the lower part of the body such as ailments of the womb.
And retained menstruation and diseases of the kidneys, chronic wounds of the thighs and legs and similar ailments. And how its removal is to be done is for his leg to enter hot water and a cloth to be placed upon it until the veins appear then to pull them above the ankle joint with the company of the vein in place at the heel clear towards the big toe.
And from it in the face of the leg there are many branches, so I removed it at its widest branch or at the heel when they come together, that is better and safer. For if you remove it in the face of the leg, he would preserve the nerves under it on the face of the leg.
And make the removal of it to be twisting as if you were going to amputate and the instrument be broken, so that the blood will not come out easily. And if the blood is hard to exit, let him put his leg in hot water and leave the blood to flow in it until it finishes, for if the remover injures the vein with the removal on the first time.
So he should remove to the top a little, for the place is safe and does not fear any evil if you preserved from the nerve as we said. And do the same with the saphenous vein of the other leg whether it be on the right or left side. But the vein of the groin remains in its place as we said at the heel from the outer side.
And the benefit of its removal for hip pain if it is from the hot blood. And how its removal is done is to have him enter a warm bath and hurry and tie his leg from the back of the hip to above the heel with four fingers in a thin long wrapper, for it will only be seen by that.
If it appears, clean it on any occasion. Either cauterize it which is better or cut it with a thread or cut it with a knife; for its place is sound, and in most people it is very small; if you do not find it.
And if it does not appear at all for the sense, clean some of its hairs and it is what appears on the back of the foot towards the ankle bone and preserved from the nerves and send as much blood as you want.
Then tie the string and put on the place of the incision a lint and tighten the place; for it will soon heal.
The 96th Chapter.
In venesection and how to use it.
The lancets can be of horns, wood, copper or glass, and venesection is on two faces, one the venesection with a condition and removal of blood, and the other the venesection without a condition.
And this venesection which is without a condition is on two faces.
And the lancets of the groins, which are two strips of the neck from both sides together, and the lancet of the chin which is under the lower jaw from the mouth, and the lancets of the shoulders, and the lancets of the glutes on the backside of the buttocks, and the lancets of the elbows, and the lancets of the legs, and the lancets of the ankles.
Venesection is only drawing blood from the veins which are spread in the flesh, and therefore it should not be let bleed as a result of cutting with an instrument nor should venesection be done with fire or without fire in any disease that results from overfullness until the body has been completely purged.
If the need arises for venesection due to an illness or in accordance with custom, we have used it at the beginning of the month and its end and at any time.
For there are people in whom, due to an excess of blood until it necessitates its removal through venesection, he finds heaviness and headache among them; some find fullness and redness on his face and head and neck among them; some find cracks on his face and genital area, dimness and darkness in his eyes; and some find a spot on the place of his incision.
And among them are those whose laughter is excessive; and some find the taste of blood in his mouth, and his gums bleed and he spits out blood; and among them are those who sleep a lot; and among them are those who, while sleeping, see blood, redness, slaughter, wounds, and what resembles that. When have we seen anything like that?
And especially if it is in the middle third of the month, we have been commanded at that time to perform venesection after two or three hours of the day has passed; and as for the benefit of cupping, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water that rises into the eyes.
But it should be after the body has been thoroughly purged; and this venesection may serve as an alternative to letting blood from the neck-veins, and we warn against using it for someone whose brain is cold or who has a headache, for it will harm him with great damage.
Therefore it should not be used by the elderly or someone whose head is afflicted with cold diseases, and by someone who has become addicted to it, for it has caused forgetfulness in him.
Therefore it should be ordered of the cupper that he lower his hand with the cup a little downwards, out of fear of causing forgetfulness; and as for the venesection of the groin, it is an alternative to letting blood from the eye-veins and the nasal vein.
Therefore, it is beneficial for phthisis and shortness of breath, constriction of the respiratory apparatus, coughing and fullness, and it should be raised with a cupping glass on the knee slightly because if it is directed downwards it has caused weakness in the heart and stomach.
And as for cupping the hollows, it is beneficial for pains occurring in the head, headache, toothache and strangulation and discomfort at the roots of the teeth, and it replaces bleeding from a purpura. It should be ordered that the cupper not deepen his hand with the condition lest he cut a vein and cause hemorrhage.
And as for cupping beneath the collarbone, it is beneficial from ulcers in the mouth and decay of the gums and similar diseases that occur in the mouth, and it replaces bleeding from a foul odor in the cheeks. And as for cupping on the armpits, it is beneficial from the palpitation which occurs from fullness and heat.
And as for cupping on the fleshy part of the abdomen, it is beneficial because bleeding from the three thin veins, which are the purpura, black bile and yellow bile, is more effective. For it draws blood from all these superficial veins in the flesh and draws those veins from other thicker ones until the attraction reaches the three thick veins.
It should be ordered that the cupper does not deepen in the condition because the place is known from the flesh and beneath it are nerves and vessels. And as for the single cupping which cups on the testicles, it is beneficial from hemorrhoids and wounds of the lower parts.
It should be ordered that the cupper makes the cup large and of copper because the place needs a strong cauterizer, perhaps the glass cup has broken and caused many bleeding. And as for cupping on the legs, it reduces fullness in a clear reduction.
Because it attracts blood from all the body and benefits those who have chronic pain in the kidneys, womb, and bladder, and regulates menstruation, and benefits those with pustules and boils. It serves as a substitute for the lancet of the venesection canals, except that it overexerts the body greatly and causes dizziness in most people, and the spines of the venesection canals have a benefit similar to the benefit of cupping the legs.
The manner of placing the spines, that is, the cup or vessel should be placed first empty and sucked with a moderate suction, without prolonging the application but rather quickly placing and removing it so that the organs rush to the place in an even way. And you should continue repeating this and following it until you see the place has reddened and opened up.
And if the redness of the blood appeared, then you should suck and continue sucking gently at first, then examine the condition of the bodies. And as for those people whose flesh is thin and loose in pores, it is necessary that you only apply one cup not more so that the place does not get ulcerated.
And the cupper orders that the cup be expanded and deepened a little, and the suction be adjusted gently and softly. And if there is in the blood thickness, it should be sucked twice, the first time to open a way for the clear blood and its moisture, and the second time to extract the thick blood.
If the blood is very foul, the cup should be repeated a third time to reach its purpose. And in general, if we want to extract little blood, one cup is sufficient, and if we want to extract much blood, more cups are used, and if we determine that the blood is thick.
So it is necessary to suck with a deep cup and the moderate level of suction in the depth of the skin only. As for what should be used from ointments when placing the cups, and from water, and what the patient should beware of, but as for those whose skin is thick, hard, stubborn, and has narrow pores.
The places of the incisions should be moistened with open, ground and pulverized ointments. If it is in summer time, then like applying saffron or violet oil or sweet pomegranate peel oil or rose oil.
And if it is in winter, then like applying narcissus oil or sycamore oil or jasmine oil or lily oil and the like. And if the gangrene is thick and cold, then let the ointment be dragon's blood oil or antimony oil or balsam oil or absinthe oil and the like.
If the incised is wide and the flesh swells, then let the ointment be omitted. And these should have their incisions washed with rosewater or cold water or foxberry water or pomegranate water or mandrake water and the like. And as for him whose blood is very moist.
So he washes his incisions with vinegar or apple and sumac water and the like. And as for him whose gangrene is thick, he washes his incisions with old wine or dragon's blood water or absinthe decoction or jasmine decoction and the like. And it should be warned to bleed in the bathroom and after the bath.
Rather it should be used after leaving the bathroom by an hour or two hours. And no one should sleep after bleeding, what should be prepared for the incised and bandaged before and after the bloodletting.
It is necessary to look first, if the incised or bandaged is bilious and the tendency of his blood is sharpness and inflammation then he should take refrigerants such as pomegranate and henna with vinegar and lettuce and saponaria and onion and the like. And let his food be bland and sheep meat crushed and cooked, cooked dates and the like.
If his temperament is cold, he should be given a drink of honey and milk or the boiled sap of the senna plant. He should also take sweet wine in moderation, that which falls between the old and the new, and he will be ordered to eat little food, making his diet birds' eggs, chickpeas, and pigeons, white pigeon meat. On the day of bleeding and bloodletting, it is necessary for him to drink more than he eats.
And it is necessary for some people to drink the Faruq potion or musk medicine or shiltha at times before bloodletting and phlebotomy, or afterwards, to strengthen the main organs and purify the blood. And it should not be given to those who are exhausted.
And as for the venous cupping that is without restriction, it is the one placed on the liver and spleen and breasts and belly and navel and place of the kidneys and the hip joint because these organs do not tolerate bleeding on them. But rather what is meant by it is to draw blood from organ to organ.
We place the cupping glass on the breasts in a case of rheumatism or we use it to relieve the member by making it breathe cold air so that the blood stagnates in the member, just as we put the cupping glass on the belly and navel; it makes the member relaxed and warm, and goes away with the pain thereby.
And it is placed on the kidneys if there is a stone or gravel in them, so that by its strength it may draw it out and perhaps open the stone or break up the gravel from its place. And similarly it does when placed on the liver and spleen when they have been confused with bad humors. And these cups are sometimes used empty with just powder.
And it is used with fire, and it may be used filled with warm water for diseases of inflammation. By filling the cup with hot water alone or with water that has been boiled in which some herbs suitable for that purpose have been cooked.
Then it is placed full on the place and held fast and left in that state, and it is reapplied as many times as necessary, and this is the image of the compress used with fire:.
Its opening is two open fingers on what we have depicted and its depth is half a fingerbreadth, and in the side of it is a small hole for about as much as a needle can enter. It is made from thick yellow copper or Chinese copper.
A flat, smooth compress so that it does not harm the part when it is placed on it. And in the middle of it is a copper or iron wire where the candle is put with fire. And this compress can be made large, larger than what we have described, and small according to the diseases.
This wire is used, for the cauteries of children and infants are not like those of men and women. And as to how this compress is put with fire on the part, it is that you take a thread well burned from linen or a small candle from tallow and place it on the middle of the pin fixed in the middle of the compress.
So the fire rises to above, up towards the top of the compress, lest it should burn the body of the cauterizer. Then it is placed on the part and a finger on the hole that we mentioned until we hold the compress, then we do not need to remove the finger.
And the steam came out through that hole and the compress dissolved on its place. Then you take the thread again and do it over if needed. And as for the compress which is used in the disease of hydrocele with water, there is no pin fixed nor a hole in it.
And it is only used by being filled with water and placed on the part alone. And this is its picture:.
And this compress, whenever it was large so as to hold a lot of water, was better,.
The Seventh and Ninetieth Chapter.
On the removal of the calcified membrane from the eye of a man who has had conjunctivitis.
This is used in most cases on the organs which cannot be grasped, either because of their small size like the cheek and lips and similar things, or because they are covered with flesh like the finger and nose and similar things.
And the way of using it is that you take from the pure water free from impurities and leave it for a day and a night in sweet water until it becomes dry, and nothing remains inside it. Then the body is purged first by venesection or bloodletting, then the affected organ is cleaned.
until it reddens, then the [alk] is placed on it. If it swells, it falls off. And if possible, the area should be bandaged with a cloth; but if it swelled and bled after falling off and this was a bad sign, then a linen cloth should be dipped in cold water.
and it is placed on top until the discharge ceases. If much blood comes, powdered saffron or alum or something similar from the pomegranate rinds should be sprinkled over it until the bleeding stops; or an extract of aloe half-cooked is put on the affected area.
and it is left until the aloe becomes sticky in the area; then the bleeding stops. And if it is necessary to reapply the alk, do not reuse that alk if possible; and if the alk does not adhere, wipe the area with raw blood.
or a needle is inserted into the area until something comes out of the blood, then it is placed; for if it feels anything from the blood it adheres to its place. If you want it to fall off, sprinkle some salt or salt or ashes on it; then it falls off from its place.
The Third Book.
For fractures.
This book is also a necessity for the craft of medicine, which is fixing breaks and setting the displaced bones.
Know, my sons, that this book may be claimed by the learned physicians and the common people, those who have never browsed through a book of their ancestors nor read one letter from it.
Because of this reason, this art has become nonexistent in our land, and I have never come across a satisfactory practitioner. And I have benefited from it only as much as I have read their ancient books with great zeal and understanding until I extracted its science, then I followed practice and experience throughout my life.
And in this book, I have recorded for you all that my knowledge encompasses regarding it. And I have experienced it after having simplified and shortened it for you, clarifying its explanations to the utmost clarity, and drawing in it many images of the instruments used in it.
As is with the increase of clarity as I have done in the preceding chapters, and there is no power except by God the High, the Great.
The First Chapter.
In it are concise and summarized statements about the matter of fractured bones, which is to be given precedence. Before we begin mentioning each broken limb separately, it is necessary that in the beginning of this chapter we mention several general sentences and sections. You are compelled first to understand them and establish their truth, you who are eager to learn this noble craft.
I say that when a fracture or displacement or sprain or dislocation occurs, one must hurry first to remove the obstacle or to straighten it or both. If a hindrance such as weakness of strength or if what occurred was something like that, it being a young boy or an old man, were to prevent that, then the time being extremely hot or extremely cold.
Then he restricts his food to cold legumes, the flesh of birds, and fish. He forbids drink, thick meat, and overeating; any food that fills the veins with blood until the swelling has subsided even if fluid is expected in the place.
Then the patient should return to his usual treatment. If he takes crushed bone in a poultice, the patient should be fed foods that provide nourishment rich and thick and soft; such as barley, rice, heads of wheat, and calf's trotters. And eggs, dried fish, and thick drink of this kind; for by this treatment the union of the fracture becomes faster and better. Know that if a fractured bone is in young or elderly men, it cannot be joined together perfectly again at all.
The bones of old men are smooth and brittle, and the bone that is soft as a result of the fracture can become like the bones of young children. But nature produces on the broken bone from all sides something similar to glue; it thickens until it holds them together.
And binds some of it to others until it comes back in a state of great strength and security as it was initially, so that nothing hinders its functions.
And for this reason it is necessary to make the patient's food consist of nourishments that are strong, solid, and thick as we have said. Know that the type of fracture differs according to the difference in organs; a break of the bone of the leg is different from a break of the bone of the head, and a break of the bone of the chest is different from a break of the bone of the back.
And also all the other organs are different from one another; and we will come to mention each type of fracture separately and in detail, without mixing them. And the type of bone fracture may differ within itself as well.
Because it may be a crack that has splintered without any fragments being formed in it, and it may be a break along the bone with fragments that are clean and unclean, and it may be a break accompanied by an incision or gash in the skin, and it may be a slight dislocation.
For each type there is a special trick in setting it, as will be mentioned separately in its place. And what indicates that the bone has broken are its deformity and bulging to the touch and its rattling when you tap it with your hand.
So if there is no visible deformity, nor rattling, nor does the bone feel disordered when you touch it with your hand, nor does the patient complain of much pain, then there is no break but rather a small sprain or slight displacement, so you should not move it at all by stretching or tapping.
But instead load it with the medicines that will be mentioned later, as they suit the place, then pull it gently, and know that if the bone has splintered and shattered into two without fragments being formed in it, except that all parts have been separated from each other.
So you should hasten to bind and straighten it before a hot abscess forms, if a hot abscess does form, then leave it for some days until the abscess settles down, then repair it in any way that is merciful and clever.
And know that setting and straightening it is easier than the bone which has fragments in it, then bind on what will be mentioned later. And if the broken bone has fragments in it, you must stretch the injured limb with both hands or legs.
Either with your hand if the limb is small, or with two cords, or with a cord and hand, and let the injured limb be placed in its natural shape on a level surface until you extend one part of the broken bone, then reduce those fragments back to their places in any way that is clever and merciful.
Be meticulous in your effort so that you do not cause the patient pain or discomfort with your action. Try to reunite one of the bones with its fellow in the best possible manner, and at this time it is necessary for you to feel both of them with your hand.
