If you see anything contrary to nature among them, try to correct it to the extent of your ability.
Be careful of severe traction and strong probing, as many ignorant people do, and often cause a severe or intense burning in the part as you may have seen from their actions repeatedly.
Then bind the limb after settling and fixing it with the desired firmness, and warn the patient that he should move it in his wakefulness and sleep, at its turning and commotion, and at his urination and all of its movements until his strength permits that the cure of the part will be safe from pain.
For whenever he feels in the state of its setting pain or discomfort, let him change it to another setting from which he does not feel pain, and while doing so let him try that the new setting be straight and even.
So that there may not be deformity in the part if it becomes stiff, and as for how to tighten the broken limb, it is as I have described to you. Know that the broken limbs differ in their smallness and largeness and smoothness, so whatever among them are smaller like the arm and finger and shin and similar ones.
So the bandages of the limbs that are smooth should be soft and flexible, but whatever among them are rough like the thigh and back and chest, it is necessary for the bandages to be hard and stiff. Because a wide strip is suitable for a large member and tightens it evenly from all sides without any defect.
Then after you have finished smoothing, start to apply the plaster that suits it. In a binding strip on the place of fracture, then begin to twist the strap on the place of the fracture itself three twists or four as the member deserves, and hold your hand lightly with the strap then take it to the upper side of the place of fracture.
With a pull less than your pull to the place of fracture, then diverge by twisting from the place of fracture slightly and slacken the pull slightly until you take something good from the correct place then take another bandage and twist it also on the broken place.
Twists then take the twist to the lower side of the fracture, and your action in tightening and slackening the twist as mentioned in the first upper twist, then put between the bandages from the binding strip or fabric whatever matches the bend of the fracture.
If there is a bend in it, and otherwise do not put anything in it, then twist another bandage over it, then fix on these tight twists as of your time if there is no swelling nor hot abscess in the member.
If there is swelling or hot abscess in it, then put on him what calms that abscess and makes the swelling go away, and leave him for a few days, then tighten on him these twists when you are sure, and let the twists be of split reeds which have been carved with wisdom, or the twists be of willow branches.
the one that is made from cypress wood or palm fronds or reeds or similar things, and let the width of each bandage be like this shape and this form specifically.
But let the bandage that is placed on the fracture itself be a little thicker and wider than the other bandages, and as for the length of the bandage it should be made according to what suits the fractured member in its size and smallness.
Then tie with another bandage on the ribs, as tightly as the first one. And tie from above with strong threads, as mentioned regarding the pulling, that it is pulled tighter on the place of the break. And whenever you move away from the fracture the pulling becomes less.
And the threads should be of moderate thickness and texture, and they should be made of wet linen in particular. Because the threads if they were thick like what I have seen from the actions of the ignorant make their threads from unfolded linen strips which is a great mistake.
Because the pulling with them goes outside of moderation. And also thin threads are not suitable because you cannot pull as much as you want with them, and it is not suitable that there be less than a finger between the bandage and the other bandage.
So if the wound becomes sore around the edges of the bandages after being pulled in the correct places, then put some linen or wool under it so that nothing will bother him from that. And as for the fracture accompanied by a wound and it has torn through the skin, we will mention that separately.
And know that not every broken limb should be tied with bandages from the first day. That is because if the limb was large, it should not be put on him unless after five days or seven or more, as you see fit to prevent the warm swelling.
And this is the quality of the dressings that were used by the ancients which are put on the break and the jaw and the swelling, a general simple dressing that cures the break and is suitable for most bodies especially children and women because it does not cause either heat or cold.
And it is that you take the flour dust which is the bread of fine wheat flour that adheres to the sides of the millstones when the mill is in motion, and you knead it as it is without whitening it with white and make its dough neither thick nor thin then cook it.
Quality of a plaster for setting fractures, extractions and ulcers. It is taken from the horse's hooves, ox hooves, aqua calamina, gum arabic, plantain, and sugar, each ten drachmas; myrrh and saffron, each five drachmas; from the mulberry tree twenty drachmas; and from Armenian or Roman clay twenty drachmas. The whole is pounded and mixed with the juice of the mulberry tree or egg white if the temperament of the patient is deranged; then this plaster is used, for it sets the broken bones quickly and is beneficial to most people because of its moderation.
Quality of another plaster which also benefits for fractures and ulcers. It is taken from plantain, horse hooves, white hogs' snout, each ten drachmas; myrrh and saffron, each five drachmas; aqua calamina six drachmas; Armenian clay twenty drachmas. All are pounded gently and mixed together and kneaded with water or egg white and used.
Quality of a plaster for joints and dislocated bones and to repel pain and prevent dislocation of the bone and fracture. It is taken from clean wool which is immersed in vinegar and cooked oil, and it is put on the place; this plaster does not have the power of setting.
But it is excellent in soothing the hot swelling and repelling pains, especially. Quality of another plaster which sets the broken bone. It is taken from fresh fig leaves and fresh mulberry leaves; they are pounded altogether and moistened with them. Quality of another brief plaster which is used for setting a fracture suddenly.
And if you want to get rid of the rest of the swelling, take a part from white hogs' snout and pennyroyal and pansy and knapweed; pound them altogether and knead with varnish if the organ is not inflamed; but if it is inflamed, knead with water of celery or with water and use.
The form of the ointment, which is stronger in analysis than this first one, is used when a hard tumor occurs at the joint of the bone; it is taken from the root of the hawthorn, and the size of vetch, the umbilicus, the king's crown, the pomegranate peel, and hibiscus from each of them a part.
They are all pounded and mixed with the water of contradiction or sweet water or paint, all that according to the temperature and quietness of the member, and as for Galen, he did not mention in his book that it is put on the broken member during its reduction.
But the ointments made from wax and oil alone, and it should be of intermediate thickness between firmness and softness, and as for Galen, he saw that the things with a skin having some heat such as resin, storax, and frankincense, and similar to what we mentioned.
The form of the ointment that benefits for weakness and pain, it is taken from dates, carob, human pubic hair or bird's feathers, hawthorn and salt in equal parts; it is pounded and sifted and mixed and applied with it, and as for how much the bandage should remain.
Then it is allowed to be removed; if not, then severe pain and inflammation or movement of the broken bone from its place should occur. If this happens in the place then a strong inflammation or distressing pain or swelling.
So, hasten and remove it in time; do not delay that at all. And clean the dressing from it then take a green cloth or a moist sea sponge and immerse it in warm water and wash with it the place until the inflammation subsides and the pain settles down then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then place on it the woolen fabric soaked in vinegar and oil or rose ointment and tie it up for a night until the warm swelling subsides and the member's inflammation goes away and its pain disappears then return it to the gentle tension and light dressing, do not bind it with your first tight binding.
If the swelling subsides so that it disappears, then you see that the tumour and redness and pain and puffing up and all the symptoms have gone away entirely and you had to apply a poultice and bandage, do it as you did at first, whether this is so or not; if nothing of what we mentioned occurs in the member.
Do not cover it except after three days or four or five or seven, and sometimes leave it twenty days altogether, as you will see the condition of the member is like what we have said until if the bone fracture is pressing and the flesh starts to clot on it.
Also add in the bandage more than what you added first, and also increase the thickness of the patient's food as mentioned earlier. If you see that the site of the fracture has become dry and rougher than it should be, know that the nourishment is unable to reach it.
Then bathe him with warm water each time you cover it, let that be every three days. Loosen the bandage a little, for this act lets nourishment reach the member and it heals quickly. And as for what foolish people who are unaware make from breaking the member again.
For if it did not heal properly and became crooked, that is a grave error on their part. And if it was correct, they would have mentioned it at first in their books and carried it out, and I have not seen anyone among them with any effect of that. And the truth is that one should not do this.
The Second Chapter.
In fractures of the skull that are open.
The types of open fractures in the head are many and their forms varied, and their causes disparate. Some fractures occur from a blow with a sword and it can be that the bone heals all together until it reaches the peritoneum which is under the bone. Just like cutting in wood.
And therefore this type of fracture is called qadumi and it can be that the sword has cut some bone and turned its face but did not extend to its end, and this type of fracture is called simply qal'a. And the wound of these two fractures is either large or small.
And there is a type of fracture which is brittle or cracked and it occurs because of being struck with a stone or falling on one, or something similar. And this type of fracture will be either that the bone has been split close to its membrane or it will be in the face of the bone.
And the wound of these two fractures is also either large or small. And there is a type of fracture that is hidden in the bone on the crown of the hair and it is a slight comminution.
And therefore this type of fracture is called sha'ri, and from it a fracture occurs because of a stone falling or being dropped and similar to it entering into the plate of the bone inside and becoming ulcerated like lead if it is struck and a part of it enters inside.
And most of that is in the fresh bone of the head like the skulls of children. And there can be splinters of all these types of fractures exposed and unexposed, and we will come to mentioning their treatment in its place, and we identify all these types of fractures by exposing them.
And examining it with probes and extracting the rotten flesh from it. And as for the hair-like type, it is identified by exposing the bone and wiping and staining on it with lamp-black, then the fracture will appear black. And as for the treatment of the fracture, one first looks at the symptoms of the patient.
If you see from his symptoms something that is clearly indicative of fear, such as vomiting bilis and hematemesis, the going out of consciousness, the cessation of voice, coma, high fever, and congestion in the eyes and redness and similar symptoms, then do not approach him or treat him because death has become inevitable for him in most cases with these symptoms.
If you see symptoms, let them not trouble you and wish for his safety. Then take him to treatment, that is if the injured person comes to you at first after being wounded and it was in the days of winter.
It is necessary to exert oneself in removing the bone before the fourteenth day, on all accounts, and if it was in the days of summer it is necessary to hasten in removing the bone before the seventh day, so that the membrane under the bone does not spoil and these symptoms which we have described are exposed.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and it was swollen and inflamed, it is necessary to cut off the broken and inflamed part as I have described to you, which is to shave the head of the injured patient.
And expose the bone in any way you can, and according to the shape of the wound, and what is hidden from the patient, if there is bleeding or a hot swelling exposed when exposing the bone, then treat it as needed.
And fill the place with poultices soaked in wine, fat and rose oil, and dress the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding stops. Then take to consolidating the bone and extracting it, this will be one of two ways of proceeding.
But the single way is that you cut the bone with a thin, narrow-bladed saw and this is its figure:.
Then use another saw after this saw with an exit slightly wider than the entrance and this is its figure:.
Then you also use another saw, slightly wider than the second one. It is necessary that you have a number of different saws, some being wider and others shorter than them, and with very sharp edges, made of Hind or good steel.
And use a hammer to strike the saw, so that the head does not shake and is harmed. If the bone is strong and hard, it is necessary to drill around it before using the saw with the drills which are called non-corroding drills, but they were named non-corroding drills.
Because it does not exceed the limit of the cranial bone's margin from behind, so that the drill has a curved mark on what is below its pointed head, similar to a necklace or small circle preventing it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
It is incumbent upon you to take several of these drill bits, each suitable for the thickness of that bone until it prepares for every skull a drilled amount corresponding to the pointed end in length and shortness on the measure of that skull's thickness. And this is an image of three types. Of drill bits, large and medium and small:.
As for the method of drilling around the broken bone, it is that you place the drill on the bone and direct it with your fingers until you know that the bone has penetrated, then transfer the drill to another place and leave after each hole a distance equal to the thickness of the rod or approximately.
Then cut with the saw what is between each two holes and do that as much as you can be lenient on it, just as we said until the bone comes off either by hand or with something else from some of the tools you have prepared for this.
Like the smooth rasp and small chisel, you should be very careful that the drill or saw does not touch anything of the peritoneum. If the bone is loosened and separated from the peritoneum if it was stuck to it and removed, then you should clean it.
And you should smooth out the roughness that remains in the bone with another tool that resembles the saw but it is necessary that it be finer and softer than all the saws, if there remained something of the small bones and splinters then take them gently with what you have from tools.
Then you treat the wound after that with lint and ointments that its mention comes, and as for the other side of the work it is a very easy face far from harm mentioned by Galen and praised greatly. This is what he says. You should first expose the part of the bone at the place where it was broken more strongly and more frequently until you have exposed that part. If you have uncovered this part, then you shape a bar with the reed and place its tip underneath it and this is its appearance.
The smooth part of it is soft and does not cut anything, while the sharp part of it in its going length as you see. The smooth part will be attached to the peritoneum and the face of the sharp part in the bone then a blow is struck on the sharp part from one side.
With a small hammer until it cuts all the bone gently as it turns and you are safe from the peritoneum so that no terrible incident occurs in it. Even if the surgeon is one of the most ignorant and strongest people, yes, even if he is careless.
If something of the peritoneum remained necessary for the bone in some places of the bone, then shake it off from it with the tip of the smooth part of the reed itself and relieve him of it gently. Then he will be relieved of it without any harm or fear.
And as for if the bone fracture did not reach to the peritoneum and that which was cut from the bone had a roughness and shreds in it, then you should strip off that roughness and remove those shreds with smooth splints which have been taken from them of different strengths so that you can use them wherever you find them most suitable and better according to what guides you to do.
And similarly the broken bone and its shape, it should be used first in stripping off the bone with the widest splint then use one thinner than that and you continue to do so on this way until you reach using the thinnest and finest of them all.
And as for the other small hair-like cracks and slight fractures, it should be treated in each one according to what leads to its recovery. It is something that does not conceal itself from whoever has a bit of knowledge in this craft and it stops at what we have written and clarified in the large fractures.
If the membrane is left uncovered when removing the bone, it should be taken a linen cloth of the size of the wound and dipped in rose oil and put on the mouth of the wound. Then another folded or crumpled cloth should be taken. And it should be dipped in syrup and rose oil and put on the first cloth, and it should be done as lightly as possible lest it burden the peritoneum. Then a broad bandage should be used and not pulled tight except for what holds the wound only.
And leave it with this treatment for a day or two until the swelling cools down, then cover it and use after that medicines which harden like salsify roots, and powder of rush, and coriander seed, and plantain, and similar things. And it should be made into an ointment from these medicines.
And scatter on the wound as it is dry, and in general use in that a medicine which will cleanse but not corrupt. And make an effort while treating your wound to have it clean, without fat or grime, nor let pus accumulate in it at all.
Because pus if it accumulates on the membrane of the brain corrupts and spoils it, and a great evil occurs from that to the patient. And it may cause in the suture of the head when the bone is exposed, and especially if it is neglected in its treatment.
And if its surface is black, look closely at that because it will be exposed to the symptoms we mentioned. Then know that he is doomed without fail. And if the blackness occurred because a medicine was put on him and the strength of that medicine caused this blackness.
So take from the honey a part and from the rose oil three parts, mix them well and smear them on a cloth then put it on the peritoneum. Then treat him with kinds of treatment until he is cured.
The Third Chapter.
For the treatment of a broken nose.
Know that the nose does not break except its upper passages, either one or both of them, because they are hard, and the lower part is made up of cartilage which does not break but becomes inflamed, contused or crushed.
If one of its passages is broken, it should be inserted into the nostril a small finger to straighten that break from inside with your thumb and index finger from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape, this being done gently and cautiously so as not to cause further pain or damage.
If the break is at the upper part of the nose and you have not joined it with your finger, then it should be straightened by the edge of a stick that is slightly thick, if the break is in both places do like this, and begin to treat it on the first day of the break.
If possible, otherwise after seven or ten days when the inflammation has subsided, then insert into the nostril a thread of linen yarn if the break is in one place, or two threads if it is in both places.
And let the thread be as thick as will fill the nostril, and some of the early surgeons have mentioned that it should be smeared with fat and changed every day, but I do not see this being necessary, rather the thread should be smeared in white egg mixed with powdered frankincense.
Then leave the thread until the bone has set and the cartilage has hardened, and sometimes in place of the thread a pipe made from goose quill is inserted into the nostril after it has been wrapped with linen yarn, making its blockage due to the broken nose more severe so that breathing will not be obstructed.
This is not something necessary if you want to make it or if you want to make the threads, when there is a swelling on the nose during your work warm the nose with lint or cotton wool soaked in vinegar and oil of roses or any of the ointments known as diachylon.
If a warm tumour does not appear it should be included with finely ground flour and gum tragacanth which we have mixed white of egg with, then place on top a piece of fresh linen, in no way tie the nose with anything. If the bones of the nose broke into small pieces.
Or if it is cracked, you should cut through it and take out the fragments with a tool suitable for that, then sew up the incision and treat it as one would to promote healing of wounds, and if there occurred an injury inside the nose, you should treat it with lint and use lead tubes. Until he recovers.
The Fourth Chapter.
For the treatment of a broken lower lip.
If the lower lip breaks without being accompanied by an incision, if it is broken only from the outside and has not been broken into two parts and there is a fissure to the inside then its recognition makes it easier.
If the break is in the right cleft, you should put your left thumb into the patient's mouth and likewise if it is in the left cleft you should put your right thumb and raise the fractured part from inside outwards gently.
And with your other hand from the outside of the bone control it and smooth it out, if the break in the jaw has been splintered into two then you should apply pressure from both sides to straighten it until you are able to smooth it out, if there occurred a growth in the teeth.
Or it has become disjointed, fasten what is separated from it with a gold or silver thread or linen then place the fractured lip in an oblique position, then put on top of it a folded cloth and put on the cloth a large firm splint or a piece of leather boot equal to the length of the lip. Then tie it from above in a way that is suitable for tying and agrees with its compression until it does not come apart, command the patient to take leave and be still and make his food soft gruel, if you thought that something had changed in shape in one of the faces then hasten to solve it on the third day.
Then tie it from above in a way that is suitable for tying and agrees with its compression until it does not come apart, command the patient to take leave and be still and make his food soft gruel, if you thought that something had changed in shape in one of the faces then hasten to solve it on the third day. Then you fix what has accumulated on it with a powder of the whetstone, either with white gypsum or fine semolina after removing the plaster from it and placing on the poultice a warm moist compress. As long as it adheres to that poultice and does not change for the bone's case then leave it.
Do not repair it until the swelling subsides and the fracture hardens. Often this fracture becomes strong in three weeks, but if a hot red swelling appears during that time, then make use of what we mentioned repeatedly to calm it down until that swelling disappears.
And if the fracture is with an incision, then look if a piece or pieces of bone have protruded; then clean up those pieces by pulling them out with what fits you to extract them from the instrument. If the wound's mouth is narrow, widen it with the forceps as much as you need.
Then if the pieces have been pulled out and there is nothing left, then draw a line on the edge of the wound if it is wide; otherwise apply one of the plasters that are suitable for that. And close up the wound until it heals.
The Fifth Chapter.
For repairing the clavicle if it has been fractured.
The clavicle often breaks from the front towards the shoulder end and its break can be one of three forms; either it cracks in two without any splintering, which is the easiest to repair.
Or splintering occurs in the break, which is harder to repair. Or the break is with an incision, and the procedure for this if the break is not with an incision is that you prepare two servants one of whom holds the arm next to the fractured clavicle while the other extends the neck towards the other side.
Then you straighten the break with your fingers until its shape becomes as it should, and there is no lump or roughness. If you need to extend more, it is necessary that you place under the armpit of the patient a ball of wool or cotton.
The bone should be as deep as your need. Stretch and raise the tendon, press the ball with your hand until the break is aligned as it should be. If you are unable to pull the tendon's tip outside because it has sunk deeply, the patient should lie on his back.
A medium-sized cushion is placed under the splint, and the servant presses the splint down until the bone in the depth rises upwards. Then repair the break and straighten it with your fingers.
If you feel that a splinter of the patella has broken off and is moving, then cut on it and carefully remove that splinter. If the splinter is lodged in the bone, use one of the cutting instruments you have prepared for this purpose to sever it. After placing under the patella the instrument that protects the peritoneum, which is an instrument of wood or iron and this is its form:.
It resembles a spoon with no objection and its width is according to what you need of the bone's size and smallness. As for its length, it is also according to what you are able to do and let it be with two ends as you see one end wider and the other narrower.
If the incision you made to remove a splinter of bone is wide and the inflamed swelling has subsided, then join the edges of the wound by stitching. If the incision is slight or if you fear the inflamed swelling, then increase the incision and the excised part as much as the incision.
If there is an inflamed swelling, then apply a poultice of rose water and vinegar or wine before the incision. And as for setting the bone if it was not injured nor broken, then place on the bone the dressing made from pumice powder with egg white. You place a soft bandage on it, then you put the ball under his armpit if that is needed, then take a turban that is very long and its width is about an arm's length or so. Then place a folded pad over the bandage and dressing, then take a thin splint board. Its display is three fingers in width and length as well, then it was inserted into a cloth, then that cloth along with the bandage was placed on the place of the turban which falls on the place of the break, then the turban was pulled back over the break like it turns. And on his neck and under the good armpit and under the sick person's armpit, it was folded back on each side. How did you see that the pulling tightens the break tightly, which is not hidden from you.
And the purpose of all this is that the bandage does not move away from the broken bone. And for this reason the wound should be examined every day. Whenever you see that the bandage has loosened and the dressing has come off, fix it and tighten the bandage, then make a small pillow for the patient to sleep on his back at night and place it under his armpit.
So that his arm is lifted with it, and the fracture of the humerus rises by the height of the shoulder or his arm is tied to his neck. And do not remove the bandage unless an accident occurs in the place such as a scratch or swelling for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see the need for that. Pull it tight and leave it until it solidifies and the fracture of the humerus becomes stronger and harder in twenty-eight days. And it may be in some people less than that.
The Sixth Chapter.
Chapter Six: On Setting Fractures of the Shoulder. The shoulder rarely breaks in its broad place and rather breaks into its pieces, so if it breaks in a place or splits in the middle, it is recognized by feeling. Therefore, depending on what shape the break is like is the form.
You fix it and put it back on its natural shape in all ways that you can, then take powdered pumice with egg whites and soft soap and put from above a fold of cloth then place upon it a broad bandage made of board.
A bandage is wrapped around the shoulder as much as possible, or a little wider than it. If there is pressure under the splint in any part of the shoulder, relieve that pressure by softening it with oil until the splint lies flat, then bind from above with a long scarf.
Tie tightly and secure the splint so that it does not move from its place, check the bandage every day. Whenever the bandage becomes loose, tighten it and adjust the splint if it has moved, and let the patient lie on their sound side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
After twenty-five days, if this period has been completed, loosen the bandage and you will be safe. For it is one of the bones that are not feared to break or displace, if a fragment protrudes from the bone which was buried under the skin, make an incision over it and extract it. Make what I have mentioned regarding soothing the hot swelling if anything of that sort occurs.
Chapter Seven.
For repairing a broken chest.
The chest may break in its middle and this is only rarely exposed, whereas its sides are more prone to breaking open. And one of its symptoms if the middle part breaks is that it inclines downwards and exposes itself to severe pain, difficulty breathing and coughing.
And sometimes it may rupture with blood and the fracture becomes clear in the broken bone, which is not hidden to touch. And its treatment is that the patient lies on his back and a cushion is placed between his shoulders then his collarbones are pressed together and his ribs are brought together with both hands from the sides, and he should be smoothed out as much as possible on all sides that can be done.
And press it firmly until the shape of the bone returns to what is proper, then place a compress and a poultice on it. Then put over it a splint made from a soft board of cypress or cedar wood, wrapped in a cloth, in the socks after it has been wrapped in a cloth. Then soften the bandage on the fractured bone so that it does not move. Pass the bandage over the fracture in a circular motion to the back, pass and tighten firmly. Then check the bandage at all times; whenever it relaxes, tighten it.
And if necessity requires that it be resolved, due to eating or exposure in the place or pain or swelling, then hasten to resolve it, remove the dressing and cure what appeared from it by its own remedy, then return the dressing when you see fit for that. Wash it and bind him tightly until he recovers.
The eighth chapter.
When the ribs are fractured, know that the fracture occurs in the prominent places near the back and their tips from the front; an incision is made there because they are cartilaginous and it should be known that this area is sensitive to touch with the fingers.
And treat it by straightening the fracture with the fingers on the capable side until the shape is even as it should be, then cool and bind the fractured bone with a splint if necessary. And if the rib fracture is slanting inward, it exposes to severe pain and suffering.
And it is like the croup which is exposed to someone who has a tracheotomy so that the bone may breathe and become exposed also to difficulty of breathing and coughing and severe bleeding, and this is difficult treatment; and the ancients have been cured in it with many tricks.
Among them is one who said that what the patient needs to eat should be something that produces wind and flatulence so that the stomach may inflate, expand and push the fracture out; but we dislike this lest it becomes a confirmation of the occurrence of hot abscess if it has not occurred, and if it has occurred then it increases in it and confirms it.
Some said it should be placed on the spot with a poultice, and squeezed forcefully. It is similar to measurement except that the poultice may draw in foreign matter into the wound due to its weakness. Some said that the place should be covered with wool that has been dipped in hot oil.
It becomes a swelling between the ribs, until it is full. The bandage will be level if you wrap it on a roundness, then the lesion is treated with the treatment of the cupping from food and medicine. If the patient's condition becomes very severe he cannot tolerate it. And the bone would eat through the membrane with an injurious eating, and we were afraid for the patient. So it is necessary that we incise on the place and uncover the broken rib, then put the tool under it which preserves the peritoneum, the description of which will come later, and we cut the bone gently and remove it.
Then we sew together the edges of the wound if it is large with suturing, and treat it with ointments until it heals. If a hot swelling appears during that time, take action before it becomes worse by applying a poultice in rose oil on the place and treating the lesion with what will calm the swelling from inside also.
And he lies on his side where sleep is most distant from him until it heals.
The Ninth Chapter.
For curing hernia of the back and neck.
As for the vertebrae of the neck, if it is broken and little happens to it in that way, but rather the crack occurs often. And also the ribs are like that. If one of them happens to you and you wanted to know whether it will heal or not. Observe, for if you saw that his hands had stiffened and paralyzed and he was unable to move them or straighten them or bend them. And even if you pinched them or pricked them with a needle, he did not feel it and did not find any pain in them, then know that in most cases he will not heal, he is lost.
If they move him and he feels a hardness in them, know that the marrow of the bone is safe and the patient will recover with treatment. If it was a boil on the back like that, and you wanted to learn whether it would also recover or not.
Look at his legs, if you see that they have relaxed and the same thing has happened in them, then when he lies on his back, urine and feces will come out without his intention. And if he lies on his belly, stool will come out without his intention. And if he lies on his back and intends to urinate but cannot, know that he is doomed and do not concern yourself with treating him. But if nothing like that was presented to him, the matter would be lighter, and the treatment of what happened from that would be to try to calm the hot boil.
To put on the inflamed pustules rose oil alone or with roasted egg white, to put three times a day on it until the hot boil settles. Then take one of the wet compresses and tie it on him and order him to remain still and calm.
He should not sleep except on the side where he does not feel pain, until it heals. And if something happened near the bone fragment or a thing that will heal from it, then it is necessary to cut open his skin and remove that bone. Then gather the edges of the wound if it is large by stitching, then treat with the embrocating ointments until it heals. And if the last bone of the spine broke, which is the coccyx, you should insert the tip of the index finger from the left hand into the seat.
And bind the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible and then apply a compress and bandage if needed, then tie him up.
If you felt a broken fragment inside, cut it out and remove it, then treat the wound with the treatment that has been mentioned until it heals.
Chapter Ten
In the treatment of a fractured hip.
Say what the bones of the hips are broken, if they were broken then only their breakage is that it becomes fragmented in its ends and splits in length and leans towards the inside and exposed to cold and pain in the place and numbness and paralysis of the leg from which the break occurred.
And treat it by passing your hand over it until you stand on the break, and see what shape it is in, if the break is only at its ends then straighten that break according to what preparation comes to you so that it resembles its natural shape.
If the break is in length or has bent towards the inside then place the patient on his stomach so that you can prepare to treat the break, and if you have straightened it then apply a poultice to it then put over it a splint from wood or skin and fasten it tightly.
Do not be afraid for him that the break will move or the splint will come off, and make the swelling of the wounds with what fills it so that the tightness becomes even, and order the patient to sleep on his back or on his healthy side, if there is an eruption and a hot abscess.
Stop from stretching it and splint him until the hot swelling subsides, then apply to him what soothes it as mentioned before, then return to splinting him and tightening him as is proper, if there are fragments or something has broken off from its ends in the bone then it should not be removed.
And do not touch it, rather straighten it from outside as we said and leave its tightness until it heals.
Chapter Eleven
In the treatment of a fractured shoulder blade.
The scapula is what is between the elbow to the top of the shoulder, if it has broken then splint it on one side, one way being that you take a rounded and smoothed branch of moderate thickness in this shape:
It is bound with two ligatures, then it is suspended from a high place and the operator sits on a chair. Then his broken arm is placed over the rod until its elbow becomes fixed in the middle of the bend of the rod. Then something heavy is tied above it.
Or a servant holds it down, then the doctor straightens the break with his hands together until the break is put back as it should be. Or the operator lies on his chest and his hand is tied to his neck with a ligature.
Then two servants are ordered to adjust one above the break with his hands and the other below it, and each of them holds it in its place. And if you want the pull to be stronger, a ligature is tied under the break and another on top of it, and each servant holds it towards its place.
If the break is near the joint of the arm, it should be that the ligature is under the armpit and the other under the break towards the elbow. And likewise if the break is near the elbow, it should be that the ligature is on that place and on the elbow itself.
Then the break is straightened gently without force until the break is in its proper place and well aligned, then it is tightened. If a hot swelling does not appear, it should be left until the seventh day. And a dressing of absorbent cotton soaked with vinegar and rose oil is placed on it.
Until the swelling settles, then it is tightened. The method of tightening is that a dressing is placed on the break, then a layer of new cloth is placed over the dressing, and the arm is brought back to its original position with the hand open on the armpit.
And the cloth and the tight bandage are held on the arm and shoulder so that the arm takes the place of the splints, if you are not prevented from doing so by something, or there is no change in the broken bone. But if you fear there will be a change in the bone, use the splints.
It is to put a splint on the break itself that is wider and stronger than all other splints, and to make three fingerbreadths of space between each splint. The length of the splints will be according to the break from either side increased by three fingers.
Then draw tight on the splints the pull that I mentioned at the beginning of this book.
So that your pull is tighter on the place of the break.
And whenever after the break the pull is weaker, if you see that the placing of the splints and the pull as we said it at the time of putting a cast on the limb from your side then do so.
And if you fear the hot swelling, leave the pull and splints till the seventh day as we said, then check the bandage every three days so that a lump or a blowing up does not occur in the place, or that the food may be prevented from reaching the member due to excessive tightness, correct all of that as we mentioned.
If you are certain that nothing of that will happen, then do not change the bandage except after many days, and the patient's arm will be on his back and hand on his stomach, it is placed under the armpit wrapped with a filled woolen cloth.
And check at all times, whether by day or night, lest the shape of the broken member becomes distorted or the bandage loosens, correct everything with your effort, and make the patient's food in the rank that we mentioned earlier to be gentle at first until if the bone should become strengthened.
It is necessary that his food be thickened, for it is the habit of the arm and leg to strengthen in forty days, so when this happens, it is necessary to remove [the splints] and use the bath and treat with ointments suitable for that, if the break was abundant and well-settled.
He should not be freed until fifty days or two months have passed.
Chapter the Twelfth.
For setting a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones which are called the humeri, one of them being small and located below the elbow and the other being large and situated under the small one from below. Perhaps one of them broke alone or both together.
When the small upper bone breaks, its setting is easier and its healing is quicker. But when the lower bone breaks, its fracture is severe and its healing is difficult. And if both bones break together, it is even more so, particularly if the larger of the two bones that broke is the one from which the small upper bone protrudes.
It is incumbent upon the physician, when setting it, to make his traction gentle and easy until he sets him straight. If the large bone is the one that is broken, he should make his traction stronger. And if both bones are the ones that are broken, he should make the traction very strong indeed.
And he should be placed in the shape of a hand, with his arm stretched out on a pillow, and his finger tips higher than all the other fingers, with his little finger below all the others and his thumb elevated on itself.
And let a pillow be placed beside him at an elevation so that the little finger is not troubled with a difficulty.
Then a servant of the arm is reached from below, either with his hand or with a bandage, and another servant reaches from above in the same way. Then the physician sets the bone until he puts it back to its best shape possible, and if there are fragments of the bone, he reduces them.
Make an effort to put every fragment back in its place. If a clean, exposed fragment appears and it is causing irritation on the skin and you have no desire to set it, then cut around it and extract it in the manner that we mentioned earlier. And if there is an open wound with the fracture, I have provided him with a way out.
If the treatment is taken from there, then a swelling appears in the first abscess and it becomes hot; a compress of cotton wool with the ointment made from rose petal fat and white wax should be applied to it, medium in thickness between thinness and thickness, and the compress should be tightened on it gently until the swelling settles.
And if the compress is removed, put the prepared poultice on top of the ground flour with white chalk; then take the splints and let the splint which is placed on the fracture itself be slightly more exposed and stronger. And know that in most cases there are six splints for the arm.
The fracture was in one arm or in the two arms together; then make your bandage tight on the place of the fracture more and stronger, and every time you rotate it upwards or downwards make the bandage looser a little as mentioned earlier at the beginning of this book.
And let the compress which is wrapped over the fracture be a soft and moist linen compress, not too stiff at all; and let the thread that ties it be from special medium thickness cotton as we have described. And inspect the limb and bandage after days.
And if something happens that needs to be corrected like a scratch appears in the limb, then the limb should be dipped in warm water until the scratching subsides; and the limb should not be tied tightly for a night until it relaxes, then the bandage should be tightened again.
And if the bandage has relaxed and the bone has been dislodged or something similar, then correct all of that with great effort; and also look, if the food is refusing to reach the limb because of excessive tightness, then loosen it a little and leave it for a few days until the food reaches it, then tighten it again.
And if nothing we have mentioned is exposed to the open air, then he should not be cured until twenty days or so pass; then hang the patient's hand from his neck; and let his arm be moderate in temperature; and let him restrain his effort from agitated movements, and make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture of the arm heals in thirty days or in thirty-two, and perhaps it heals in twenty-eight days; all according to the constitution and strength of the body.
The thirteenth chapter.
In setting a fracture of the hand and fingers.
The palm of the hand and the joints of the fingers are rarely exposed to fractures, but they are often exposed to incisions. When the hand or a finger is exposed to a fracture or an incision.
The surgeon should sit raised and in front of him a stool on level ground.
Then he puts his hand on it, stretched out, then the servant brings the fractured bones and the physician arranges them until they are properly set. If a hot inflammation occurs, then a poultice is taken and a splint is placed over it according to the size of the place.
And I have wrapped it in a green cloth. If the fracture is towards the bottom, towards the palm of the hand, then make a spherical shape from the cloth and order the surgeon to grasp it with his broken fist, then bind it with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of soft skin so that the skin becomes pliable with all the flexing of the hand, and tie it as needed. If the fracture is outside, then make a splint from above and another splint from below in the hand so that the hand may be open and standing upright.
Then he carries the dressing as it would be with a moving hand and he ties between the fingers with a bandage. If the fracture is on one of the joints of the finger, and it is the thumb, then let him straighten it as needed, then tie it with the hand, and if you wish to make for it a small standing splint to hold the break.
And it should not move. If the fracture is on any of the fingers such as the middle finger or the index finger or the little finger, then let him straighten it and tie with the next healthy finger or tie them all together in a bundle, that is more suitable, or place a small standing splint on them.
As we have said concerning the swelling, and inspect when you break the blister and afterwards from the hot tumour, face it with what is proper if something of that has happened as often described.
The Fourteenth Chapter.
For setting a fracture of the thigh.
The bone of the thigh is often broken and it becomes evident by touch as it shifts forward and backward. Its setting is done by pulling a strap above the break and another below the break, with the patient lying on his stomach.
Then each servant straps a ligature to its place on the middle of it if the break is in the middle of the bone, and if it is near the hip joint it should be straped with a thick ligature either of wool or like that.
When the break is near the hip joint up to about the groin, so that the pull should be upwards and the other ligature below the break. Likewise if the break is near the knee, then the ligature should be near the knee so that the pull should be downwards. Then the physician smooths out the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone becomes perfectly consolidated and when that happens, it is necessary to lift the dressing and pull tight if there is no hot tumour in the member; but if a hot tumour did happen then leave it for some days until the hot tumour settles down, then go back to its treatment.
And as for pulling it tight, it should be wrapped with a stiff and wide headband over the break two or three times leaving some of it remaining. Then wrap the leg until the heel is at the level of the ankle joint and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below so that the ends of the thread are on top from both sides.
Then turn what remains of the headband around the leg and thigh. Then make on the thigh in place of the break itself two splints which make one plaster on the bone of the leg. Then stuff the gap between the thigh and the leg with soft cotton to equalize the pull.
Then start with a strong pull from the middle on the site of the break three times or four, and every time you apply the bandage to the site of the break let your pull be less and more gentle and looser.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread which you placed between the thigh and the leg, tie them to what follows of the splints that are above it, then pass by the ends of the thread to below until they reach the anklebone, pull on both also the ends of the splints from the other side.
So that the bandage does not slip from its place, then leave tension on it as long as there is no movement in the limb, pain, swelling or inflation and so on. If anything of this happens, act to solve it and correct what happened of it all as we have mentioned many times.
If there is a splinter in the bone that protrudes, it should be smoothed out if possible. Otherwise, split on it and remove it, then treat the wound as was mentioned before until it heals. Sometimes this fracture of the thigh can be united without attaching the leg with splints.
As we mentioned in the arm and shoulder, except that splinting like this does not expose it to injury from a fall, but if she were to be splinted alone without attaching the leg to her then there is no escape for its owner except being lame forever.
Know that the thigh heals in fifty days or a bit more or less, all of that according to the difference in temperaments and other circumstances.
The Fifteenth Chapter.
In fracture of the patella (kneecap).
The patella of the knee is rarely exposed to fracture, and it may be frequently exposed to incision. If a fracture does occur, then either it will split or become cleft in its parts and this can happen with injury or without injury.
All of this is felt by the hand, and it is corrected by rearranging its scattered parts with the fingers until they come together and are united as much as possible through careful manipulation and delicacy. Then carry a dressing and place on it a rolled bandage if necessary.
She tightens from above the tension suitable for that, then she takes care of all the situations which we have described in other fractures such as hot swelling and similar things by matching each obstacle with what corrects it until he is cured.
The Sixteenth Chapter.
In splinting a fractured leg.
The leg has two bones, one thick which is called the tibia and the other thin which is called fibula, and they are exposed to types of fractures similar to those of the humerus. Therefore, its splinting is like the splinting of the arm, in one manner, whether both bones are broken the leg turns to all directions, and if only the thin bone is broken it turns forward, and if the thick bone breaks from below that will be clear to you.
So use a mold and smoothing, and binding the splints according to that, whether it be, except that if the break of the leg is a severe one with many shreds, then let the mold be less and lighter, and exert yourself in its splinting.
And in the leg there is something added to the arm's work, which is that if you have smoothed the splints and finished all your work, then take two poplar splints which are used for flattening rooms which are put between gaps of boards or are made from palm frond pages or similar things.
And choose from them what is a little thick and not of splints, let its length be along the leg from the knee to below, then wrap on each one a strip wrapped twice its length, place the one on the leg and the other on the opposite side.
Let it be from the knee down to the foot, then tie the bandages in three places at both ends and the middle. By this binding, the leg will not tilt to the right or left and will be stable in good fashion. And a stick of wood can be used as a splint along the leg.
It is placed in it to preserve him from movement. And more often, this should be done when there is a fracture accompanied by a specific wound. Then examine the leg every day and take great care of the swelling or inflation or anything else. When something of that happens, treat him with what is necessary until he recovers. The leg bone heals in thirty days or about that.
Chapter Seventeen.
For the breaking of bones of the foot and fingers.
But the heel is not exposed to bone breakage at all, and as for the foot bones, they are sometimes exposed to breaking. And also the fingers are rarely exposed to breaking but rather to a wound in most cases.
If it is exposed to bone breakage of the foot and I have seen those bones have overlapped some on some, then have the patient place his foot on the ground standing upright like walking. Then come and place your foot on what has risen from those bones and press down and knead.
Until it returns to its place, then take the bandages and splints from above and put them under the sole of the foot a small board with flat ends. Then tie the foot tightly with them after wrapping it with cloth and all that is needed.
If three or four days have passed for him, then release the bandage. You will find that the bones are aligned, whether they were broken or dislocated. The treatment is as you see it. And if some of the fingers were broken, splint and knead them according to what I described to you in the splinting of hand fingers.
Then place a splint for the broken finger along its length, and let it be slightly wider than it. Then place this board under the foot which I have described to you and fasten it tightly, if two or three or more bones were fractured.
Then place on each finger a splint of its length made from linen and fold it, then fasten the board to the bottom of the foot so that the head of each end of the board is outside the leg.
And it is advisable for you not to forget all what we have told you in case of other fractures, and face each condition with what is required.
Chapter Eighteen.
In the fracture of the thigh bone and the hip bone in women, and the male pubic bone.
When the female pubic bone is fractured, then have her sit up and bend her back a little, a little, while holding from behind, then pack the dressing into her vagina with cotton until it fills and becomes like a ball.
Then shake the woman and raise her pelvis a little, a little, because that cotton will come out until it becomes like a ball at the vulva gate and the bone fracture returns, then place a pillow on her back. When she wants to urinate, remove the cotton gently so that she can urinate.
And return it to the way I first placed it then go back to her first pillow, she does that for seven days or so because it will heal, and if you want to take a catheter, tie a tube on her mouth and insert the catheter into her vagina.
Then blow into the tube with force until the bladder swells inside the vagina, because the fracture will return, then bandage her with cotton and rest for days as we have described until she heals, and if the male or female hip bone is fractured it should be treated in a splint.
The reduction of what we have described in the thigh bone, and it will not be hidden from you that this is a rare fracture, especially for those who have some weakness. My writing here is to show mercy because the break itself usually indicates the correct procedure.
According to the correct procedure for setting and tightening, you will understand this. But if a man's thigh bone is fractured like that of a goose, then a man enters it, and a cloth or cloth strip is placed on him, compressed, and left for three days or about that until he recovers.
The Nineteenth Chapter.
In setting fractures of the bones if they were with a wound.
It is necessary for one who presents a fracture with an open wound, especially if the bone is large like the thigh or arm bones or similar to it, that you hurry and cleanse it from its time as the conditions of opening have been presented.
If the wound is bleeding blood, it is necessary that you hurry to sew it up by applying powdered alum on it if what else is available is not there then take the fracture and set it in that day itself without delaying.
If a suppurating abscess does not occur, then if it occurs leave the setting until the ninth day so that the hot abscess cools down and do not approach him in the third or fourth day at all because he will be exposed to bad symptoms.
If the broken bone is protruding on the skin and exposed, it is necessary that you try to reduce it and set it with your hand gently and a short distance, and if you cannot do so with your hand then straighten it with this tool which is made of iron seven or eight fingers in length. And place it according to the size of the wound.
Therefore, it is necessary for the doctor to make from it three or four as needed according to the treatment in each type of fracture and let them be slightly thick so that they do not bend when hammered on them during work.
Its corner will be sharp and it has an angle at its end, and the top of it is thicker while its middle part becomes very thin as it goes down. This is its form:.
It is called by the Greeks, Pyramideon, a small wedge. It should be fashioned so that its sharp end points towards the fractured bone and pushes it into place with one blow, until the bone has returned to some degree of alignment and the ends of the fracture have been smoothed out against each other.
If the fractured end is thin and the instrument has not taken hold of it properly, then the tip of that bone should be cut off so that the instrument can take hold of it. If you are unable to push the bone back into place as we have described, then cut it with the sawing pieces which we mentioned earlier.
Or spread it with one of the forceps in whatever way you can, then remove what remains inside the bone from roughness and coarse scales. If after you have put the bone back and found a very painful swelling then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
If you are able to return it to its natural place then do so, for you will be doing the swelling a great benefit. If your fracture healing is complete, then dip a cloth in black tightening wine especially if you are in summer and do not put any kind of ointment on the wound.
So that decay and corruption may not occur in it. Then use the poultices when you are free from dressing the bone and leave the wound uncovered by stitching it with a needle in linen folds to the extent of the wound.
And be extremely cautious not to tighten the wound with the fracture, for foolish doctors have done so many times and caused death or gangrene or putridity in their patients. Let your tightening of it be soft and loose, contrary to that which is done for other fractures.
If the wound is minor or it was a large wound and there were some bad symptoms we have described which worried him, and he found pain in the place unbearable, then it should not be covered with bandages. Make for him a strip of thick leather in the place of the bandages and fasten it therewith.
If after a day or two you see that the wound has begun to form pus, remove from it the leather strip we put on him with the drink then use the plaster and ointments which are our custom in treating wounds like the compound plaster and others. It is necessary for you to untie the bandage.
Examine the wound every day in the morning and evening until it heals and clears, and it is necessary for you to make a support for the limb so that the pus will flow out easily downwards. If many days have passed on the wound and the pus has not ceased from it then know that there are small splinters of bone.
You should investigate the wound with a probe, what is from those splinters that are clean then remove it and take it out, and what is not clean, was causing to eat into the limb and cause pain, burn its parts and removal thereof in any way you can.
If a fever, flux or another kind of corruption and badness appears to the wound then it is necessary that you face every one of them with what the treatment we mentioned in its place has prescribed.
And it is necessary that you stand at the words and prepare your mind if a large bone breaks and presses on the limb such as the hip or shoulder and similarly to other of the major limbs, then do not take hold of its pull nor removal. Often death is presented from that, but leave it until it becomes putrid perhaps after twenty or thirty days then it will fall off by itself.
When you see that the wound has a place for treatment, then treat it; otherwise leave it alone.
The Twentieth Chapter.
In the treatment of swelling that occurs as a result of some fractures.
This swelling often occurs after the removal of the fracture, especially when it is near a joint, and its appearance disfigures the limb, and may even prevent the limb from performing its natural function.
If the swelling is soft, then use remedies like senna and galbanum and myrrh and the roots of aloe and similar substances by taking some or all of them and mixing them with a constricting drink or egg white or vinegar.
And apply it to the swelling firmly, and bind it with a strong binding for a long time, then release it and reapply it again until the swelling disappears if God wills it; or bind over it a well-made lead plate.
For lead has a property that will dissolve everything it comes into contact with in the limbs, so if the swelling has hardened and it is necessary to remove it, make an incision above it and cut out or strip away the necrotic tissue until it disappears, then treat the wound until it heals.
The Twenty-First Chapter.
In the treatment of a fracture when it becomes bent and the limb remains soft in its place other than as was before.
If a bone fracture becomes bent and the limb remains weak after that, it is only for many reasons. One of them is that there have been many tightenings of the bandages and binding it incorrectly, or excessive tightening of the bandages to the point where nourishment cannot reach the limb, or excessive stretching and movement at an inappropriate time, or a lack of blood in the patient's body and its weakness.
To treat this, feed the patient and nourish his body until blood becomes abundant in him. And use the bath, and insert joy and happiness into him and similar things, then bear zinc on the member to attract a lot of food or to soften it with warm water. Until food is carried in it and it returns to its natural shape.
The Twenty-Second Chapter.
In the treatment of broken bones if they become misshapen and prevented from functioning as they should.
For when an afflicted member is presented with misshapening or deformation of the broken bone or complication and decay, thus changing the appearance of the member. But since the member has not been prevented from functioning naturally, it is not advisable to accept the saying of him who claims to break the member into two parts and it was done by many ignorant physicians and practitioners in our country.
And this act is very reprehensible and leads to great corruption, but if the misshapening and complication are mild, it is advisable to soak in water that has been cooked with green herbs such as dill leaves, its stem and crown and similar things.
And it is smeared with the cooked medicines like firm-made diachylon or it is taken from the salivary glands of dill and it is beaten with lard of chicken and fat of hare, and it is smeared with it, or it is taken from the thick fig and crushed with pigeon droppings and similar remedies that are called breakers of coagulation.
And the complication will dissolve with continuous gentle rubbing with the hands, and the member is moved in all directions at all times. If the misshapening has appeared and increased and become hardened and necessity demanded its treatment with iron.
So it is necessary to cut the top of it and release the connection of the bone and to remove what remained from complication or bone with smooth cuts, and to use gentleness in that with effort and care, then to treat the wound as mentioned above until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-Three.
The jawbone is a dislocation of the joint from its place, causing hindrance in movement and uglification of the body part, as well as severe pains and aches. Whenever one of them occurs, he should hasten to return it to its original position.
He should not delay, because if he delays, the place will swell and it will become difficult to return the jawbone. Therefore, he should not delay nor move him nor stretch in the time of swelling, as often a painful twitching and distressing pains occur on the wound.
But if that occurs, he should hasten to let out the abscess then leave it until the swelling subsides a little. Then, he will wet the member with hot water and oil, and return it gently, and treat each member as mentioned in its place.
And he has also arranged the chapters on the jawbone, in accordance with what preceded regarding the fracture, from top to bottom.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
For treating the lower jaw bone.
The jawbones rarely come loose except in rare cases, and their loosening is either a slight displacement from their place or a complete dislocation until they relax. Until it reaches the chest, so that saliva flows from the wound and he is unable to hold it in or put his jaw together and his tongue trembles with speech. But if the dislocation is slight, he usually returns to its original state with minimal effort.
And as for if the dislocation is complete and total, he should hasten to return it quickly without delay. A servant will hold the patient's head and the physician will insert the tip of his finger into the root of the jawbone inside the patient's mouth, if the jawbone is on one side.
If the jawbone is cracked from both sides and all his fingers are outside, he makes him bring them together and orders the operator to loosen his jaws and let it go in every direction. The doctor arranges the jawbone until it returns to its place.
If its return is difficult, especially if the jawbones are both cracked, use the plaster with hot water and grease until it becomes easy to return them. Do not delay their return as we said. If they have returned and stabilized and the operator's mouth has closed without his jaws straining then put on them wrappings of flax made from wax and grease and red dye.
Then tie him gently with a loose bandage. The operator should sleep on his back and his head should be supported between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right or left, nor do any chewing of food but make his food smooth and liquid.
Until the pain goes and the jawbone is healed, let him eat what he desires. Let him use this with gentleness and not exert himself when opening his mouth to eat or drink or speak until the jawbone heals and recovers.
If the jawbones are hard to return if they have come apart at any time and not returned to their places, it often happens from that that fevers and permanent headaches occur and perhaps his belly will be bloated and he will vomit repeatedly. If you see this then know that he has failed.
And often he dies from complications of this within ten days.
The Twenty-Fifth Chapter.
In the treatment of a fractured jawbone and the tip of the shoulder blade.
As for the scapula, it does not crack from the inner side because of its connection to the chest and it may crack outwards which can be felt. Fixing it is that the patient lie on his back and stretch out his arms.
Then press the area with your fist with a strong pressure, so it will return. Then place on it the poultice and the gauze and fasten it. And as for its end that is next to the shoulder blade and connected to it, it should not come out except in rarity. If it comes out one day, it should be returned and adjusted according to what we mentioned, and you prepare yourself with.
Then place on it the poultice and the gauze and fasten it, and command the patient to be calm and still until they recover. And with this treatment itself will return the end of the shoulder blade if it also has moved away from its place.
The Twenty-Sixth Chapter.
To return the tendon of the shoulder.
Know that the shoulder tendon may come loose in three ways: one is that it comes loose towards the armpit downwards, and the second is that it comes loose towards the chest perhaps upwards from the shoulder blade, which is rare.
It does not come loose behind the place of the shoulder nor ahead of the place of the nerve, and it is more common that it comes loose and goes out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those whose tendons are few because it exits quickly and reenters quickly.
And as for those whose tendons are many, it is the opposite, that is, it comes out with difficulty and goes in with difficulty. And perhaps some people experience a blow or a fall so the shoulder tendon becomes swollen and inflamed, and they think that it has come loose and should be checked until its safety is confirmed.
So when that happens, you proceed with its treatment. If the tendon comes loose towards the armpit, check between the detached and intact tendons, if you touch them, you will find a visible difference between them, and you will feel the head of the tendon in the place of detachment like a stone, and under the armpit when touched, the head of the tendon as if it were an egg.
No doctor should be able to lift his hand to his ear or move it in all movements, and likewise if it swells towards the chest or upwards then you will find that obvious which is not hidden.
And this swelling can be easily reduced if it was soft or the doctor a young man, to make a servant lift his hand up then you place your fingertips under his armpit and raise the joint with force upwards to its place while the servant lifts his hand and extends it upwards.
Then he lowers it downwards and it returns quickly, if it does not return as we mentioned and the swelling has been present for many days it is necessary that the doctor bathes in hot water and uses a poultice which stretches and softens like boiling a piece of mastic and a mustard plaster and a crown in water and he uses them.
Then the doctor lies on his back and you place under his armpit a ball of wool which is moderate between softness and firmness then the doctor makes his ankle rest on the ball and holds the patient's hand with his own hand and extends it downwards.
And he pushes the ball with his foot then it returns to its place, and if you want to reduce it in this way, prepare a leg taller than the patient and hold him from the side, and insert your knee under the patient's armpit and lift his arm up until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand down to his stomach, and if the patient is light then something else should be hung on him so that he lifts it, because this will return the swelling from its place, and it can also reduce in another way.
And it is that you place in the ground a long board with its head rounded like the head of a sling, not thick nor thin, then it is placed under the patient's armpit after putting a soft pad on the head of the board and the doctor stands on the length of the board.
Then he extends his hand to the lower side from the other side and also stretches his body from the other side with force, so that the joint returns quickly to its place. If its return is difficult in all that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: take a stick whose length is about two arms. And place it, as much as four fingers, and thicken it, as much as two fingers, its head being round so that it may be easy to enter the depth of the armpit in this form:.
Then it is tied to the round head a knot, that it may not injure the stick. Then he makes it go under the armpit and extends his hand entirely, and the arm on the stick down and ties the stick around the shoulder, elbow and wrist of the hand.
Then the arm is put on a support. Hold with the support and extend the hand down, leaving all the rest of the body hanging from the other side. So that the joint enters through the shoulder, if its entry has been completed in any way possible, then it should be placed under the armpit a ball of moderate size made of wool.
Then he carries the prepared poultice with dust of the millstone, with frankincense and egg-white on the shoulder all over as it turns from above. Then the ball is pulled tightly from under the armpit and tied firmly with a cord around the poultice from above and his hand is hung to his neck.
And he is left, not moving his hand for seven days. It should be that the food of the patient becomes little until the member gets stronger because it will heal faster, then after seven days or five, he exercises with movement, so if it has been established and did not weaken, then it is healed.
If the joint slips frequently due to wetness exposed to it or another injury, then he should use in it a poultice with the three saffwids as mentioned in the chapter of the poultice, if this is done and the bandage has been dissolved after seven days and the joint did not stabilize.
The poultice was reapplied and the ligature tightened on it several times, but it did not hold fast and dropped off and relaxed, nor could it be raised above. So know that its nerve which is in the head of the joint has severed or stretched out or relaxed. Know then that the joint will never remain in place again.
And as for the jaw which is towards the chest and breast, and behind it, if its parts are pulled back with force until they return, all kinds of traction and treatment are used until it heals. Then, after healing, a lump may appear in the member and movement be impeded.
So let him use the herbal hot compress repeatedly until that lump softens and returns to its natural state.
The Twenty-Seventh Chapter.
In the treatment of the shoulder joint's jaw.
The joint of the shoulder is easily dislocated and also returns easily to its place, and it dislocates in all directions, especially forward or backward. Its jaw will not be hidden from you because it is under your sight and touch in whatever shape it dislocates.
And if you compare the dislocated shoulder to the normal one, it will be clear to you by a manifest sign, namely that the joint becomes loose and the arm cannot bend nor can you touch its head with your hand. You should hasten and return the jaw to its place before it is exposed to hot poultice.
For if it is exposed to hot poultice and it becomes difficult to return, it will not even heal, especially if the jaw is dislocated backwards, for it is most difficult of all types of dislocation and most painful and many a time death comes with it.
And to correct it, if it is something that can be returned, let a servant extend his hand with both hands and his arm outstretched, the doctor's hand from above the shoulder and from below, and he pushes the joint with the tips of his fingers together or with the heel of his hand until it returns to its place.
If the jaw is forward, it can be returned by bending one's hand once so that its wrist strikes the curved part that is opposite to it. If the jaw does not return on being bent back then a very strong force should be applied and the arm stretched out by two men.
The operator is also taken by two men so that he does not fall down while the force is being applied then his arm is turned in all directions after it has been wrapped with a rolled up cloth or a wide bandage, and if the doctor of joints begins to operate with his hands, he should wipe them with oil so that it may help in the easy sliding of the joint.
Then the joint is pushed strongly until it returns, and after it has returned it should be wrapped with a poultice in which are kneaded and dried white of egg and tightened tightly, and the arm is tied from the operator's neck and left for some days.
Then he should leave it, if the joint has remained in its place then the bandage is removed from it and left like that, and if you see that the joint has not been strengthened enough then the poultice and bandage are reapplied and left for some days until it strengthens up then it is removed.
And if it is difficult in movement then the operator should use moistening in the bath, gentle washing and rubbing until it softens or he puts on the joint a fat kid skin then binds it and leaves it on for a day and night then removes it and lets him enter the bath, and when sweat appears he should rub the joint with moderate rubbing.
Then the skin is reapplied on it once, twice and thrice with entering the bath until it softens, and if you want to put on it beef gelatin moistened and hidden with fat and bind it tightly do that also several times then it will soften and return to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
In the treatment of shoulder dislocation.
The shoulder of the hand is often dislocated and its reseting is easy except that it should be done immediately when it is dislocated before redness or heat appears in the place.
Reset the shoulder by placing the dislocated shoulder on a board, having a servant stretch his hand and place the doctor's palm over the joint and push it until it returns; however, one should check if the shoulder has gone into the hollow of the arm.
Let the patient place the back of his hand on the board when pressing and releasing, and if the shoulder is prominent to the front of the arm, then let him put the back of his hand on the board so that the doctor's hand will be over the same joint; if it returns immediately, otherwise hold it with a soothing plaster for the swelling.
And leave it alone because it cannot be reset after days have passed to it, except that the joint remains inflamed and it does not harm the patient in any way unless the hand becomes swollen and unable to grasp anything.
