Therefore, learn that the nerve has been cut or crushed and there is no remedy for it except with a poultice, perhaps that will benefit, perhaps not. If the shoulder is reset then apply the plaster which we have described, then bind and leave on for five days.
Then let him unbind and exercise the hand; if its movement is difficult and something of stiffness appears in it, then soak it with hot water and alkali several times until it loosens.
Chapter Twenty-Nine.
In the treatment of finger dislocation.
Fingers can dislocate in any direction, so if a finger dislocates from the front or back of the palm, stretch out the finger and push the joint with your thumb until it returns, then bind the tip of the finger and hang it towards the side to which it dislocated.
Leave it for two days, then release and stretch it until it becomes straight. Do the same at night if it was during the day, so that she adjusts to it and trains on movement and binds it at night. Continue doing this for several days until it strengthens.
And do the same with it if it came undone to the palm of the hand, tie it in the same direction and do with it what you did first until it is freed, and also do with it whenever it comes undone to any other part.
Chapter Thirty.
The treatment of a dislocation of the back bone ring.
When a complete dislocation of one of the vertebrae or neck rings occurs, or when many of them move away from their places, there is no treatment for it because death hastens to the patient.
And a sign of that is that the patient's stool comes out without his will and he cannot hold it, and often some of his members become limp, either both legs or one of them, or both arms or one of them. And if one vertebra moves away from its place then many are likely to move as well.
And it can move away in four directions, the one that moves to the back is called a vertebra. And as for its treatment, if the displacement occurred from birth then there is no cure for it and it will not be completely healed. But if it occurred from a fall or a blow etc.,
I have mentioned these displacements previously with various treatments in lengthy descriptions, most of which do not benefit much. I have shortened what was said and simplified it for its meaning and clarified the device, contrary to what they explained and described.
I say that the vertebra which occurs in front of the chest, there is no remedy for it and no cure. And similarly the one that occurs on both sides also has no treatment, but only the one that occurs in the back, as I have described, can be treated by making the patient lie down.
On a platform next to a wall, place a moist mat under him lest he should hurt his chest. Then put a vertical beam inserted in a hole on the ground towards his head and another beam towards his feet at the other end of the platform.
A servant holds the beam and let it be loose in the hole, another servant holds the other beam in this way. Then wrap a soft and thin cloth around his chest and under his armpits, extend one end of the cloth to the beam near his head and tie him there.
Then pull with another cloth above his thighs and shins, and at his ankles. Then gather all the straps together and tie them in the other beam near his feet. Then each servant will stretch to their own end the beam with the strap and the beams will not be removed from their fixed places.
Unless they are not secure as we said, the doctor will put his hands on the pin with great force until it returns or he puts a board on it. Then lean on the board with his feet until it returns. If it doesn't return by this treatment, take a board that is three arm-lengths long.
And dig a hole in the wall near the patient as we said, a place for the end of the board. Then put the middle of the board on the peg and the doctor will put his feet on the other end and pull tightly until the pin is pressed down.
And it will return to its place, and if you want to make it with a ring that can be screwed by hand, insert into the ground near the patient's head at the end of the platform two beams one arm-length long, and two other beams also one arm-length long at the other end of the platform.
And let there be a palm's breadth between each beam, and make in each beam a hole where the ring will pass. And secure all the wood in the ground tightly so that it does not move at all. And insert a turned stick, which is the ring that turns with the strap in the holes of the two beams.
In his side is a hole that has been bored through with a rod of a span long as it bends, and in the two wooden splints similar to that; then pull the strap which you tightened around the patient's chest into the cup near their head and the straps which you tightened around their legs into the cups near their feet.
Then he stands beside each cupbearer, who fastens his hand with the turner that bends the cup, and the physician smoothes out the protuberance as we have previously mentioned. This is an image of the cupbearers, the splints, and the patient:
Then, after the splints are taken away and the place is made even, it should be carried a dried poultice with egg white, then starch, and put on top of the poultice a splint made from a board which will have a width of three fingers or more and as long as the swelling.
And on some of the correct plaster then tie with the strap that is supposed and let the patient eat soft food until he recovers, if there remains some swelling in the place after recovery it should be used remedies that are applied to soften along with the board which we have mentioned for a long time.
And it can be used in that a lead plate, and sometimes there remains a lump after the plaster is removed which will be thought to be a bone abscess and it may indeed be an excess bone that has been swollen, do not treat it with this treatment for perhaps death would result.
The thirty-first chapter.
In the treatment of the dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the shoulder are only exposed to dislocation, and are not exposed to what other joints are exposed to, such as slight slipping or falling out, and the joint of the hip is dislocated on four sides.
This is because it dislocates towards the inside and outside, and ahead and behind, and more often dislocates towards the inside, and less often towards the front or back. A sign that it has dislocated inward is that if you compare the healthy leg of the patient with the affected one, the affected one will be shorter.
The knee will be more flexed than correct, and the surgeon cannot bend his leg at the armpit. And the place that follows the armpit is warm and clear, before the head of the thigh has reached there.
And the sign that the jaw is exposed to the outside will be against these symptoms, and the sign that the jaw is exposed forward will relax his leg completely except that he cannot bend it without pain. If he tries to walk, he will not be able to do so in front.
And he will retain his urine and tremble at the armpit. And when he walks, his heel will be on the calcaneus. The sign that the jaw is exposed to behind is that he cannot flex the knee nor can he bend it before the armpit bends. His leg will also be shorter than the other.
And the armpit is relaxed and the head of the thigh is at the place of the patella clearly. And as for the return of this type of fracture, if you look, then if the fracture is old it has aged with its owner and did not try to restore it and remained in its state, then there is no cure for it at all.
Then it should not be treated. And as for the one whose fracture is new and was of any type, whether from the four faces of the fracture, then proceed that the joint will flex and stretch it inward and outward and move it to right and left, perhaps.
He will recover and did not need any other treatment. And if he does not recover like this, then it should be to bind a strong man with his arms or else with a stick that is tied on his leg above the knee, and another servant to lift him from above.
That his hands will enter from under his armpits then tie with a soft stick on the trochanter and another servant holds the end of the stick, a third servant to lift him either from in front towards the axilla to the patella or else from behind towards the back.
All of them are stretched together until the splint rises with the body from the ground and remains suspended. If this type of stretching is used for a fracture of the jaw, as we mentioned earlier, or else each type will need its own specific treatment.
As for the treatment specific to it, if the jaw is dislocated inside, then the splint should be placed on the correct side of the body. Then a bandage or a cloth strip should be wrapped around the point where the femur meets the pubis.
Then the bandage should be extended upwards from the pubis to the top of the body, towards the armpit. Then another servant will take hold with both arms and embrace the swollen area from the upper thigh. He will extend it outwards with great force and it will return to its place.
This type is easier than the other types of treatment by which this member can be returned. If it proves difficult for you and you cannot resort to this type of treatment at all, then the servant's legs should be tied with a strong bandage, soft on the ankles and on the knees.
Then it will be so that each one of them is tied with the strength of four fingers. The upper leg should be stretched more than the other by two fingers, then the splint will be hung on a beam in the house and it will be above the ground by the length of an arm.
Then order a strong servant to hold the upper thigh and have another servant hang onto the splint. Then push the other servant who is holding the thigh with force and the joint will return to its place quickly. As for its specific treatment if the jaw is dislocated outside.
Then the servant should lie down on a couch as we described for the treatment of a hernia and the bandage should be tightened tightly around his upper leg and chest. Then two boards should be placed under his feet and the other two under his head. Then an additional board should be placed in the middle of the couch, very firmly secured.
She is wrapped with a damp cloth so that she may not hurt the patient, lest her splint be between his thighs and when he bends down it will slide off. Then every attendant goes to his place and the doctor straightens out the hip with his hands, if it responds well to the returning.
Otherwise, place the board on him and tie it as mentioned in the thigh, provided that the patient's back leans against a correct shoulder. If the deformity is forward then stretch one of the patient's legs once he is on this state towards the opposite side.
The doctor places the back of his right hand on the upper thigh and presses it with the other hand, yet makes the pressure extended downwards towards the knee. If the deformity is behind then the patient should not be bent down.
And he is raised above the ground but rather should be placed on a hard thing, as it also should be from its bend and knee outward according to what we mentioned of his bending down and his face upwards with the bandages tightened.
As we said earlier and it is also necessary to use the board compress on the place from which the joint has come out, If the hip joint returns as needed and its return sign is not hidden from you and he stretches his legs.
If you see them steady and the patient lifts and lowers his leg without difficulty, know that the member has returned as it should. Then connect the thighs to each other and take the splint and tie with a strong bandage so tightly that the hip does not move in any direction.
The patient requires rest for three days or four, then the bandage and splint are removed and his leg is measured with a straight line. If you see them equal in quantity know that the joint has been secured. So release the patient to walk, but if you see something relaxed in it.
Return and bind it (the fractured bone) tightly and strengthen it according to the strength of your first bandage, then leave it for three days. Then remove it. And it will gradually walk on it until its strength returns, God willing.
The Second and Third Treatises.
In the treatment of a broken knee.
The knee can break in three ways, it breaks inward and outward and downward meaning towards the back. And there is no break to the front at all, and a sign of its breaking is that the patient orders his surgeon to bind his leg to his thigh.
If it does not bind to the thigh, know that the knee has broken. Treat all three breaks, have the surgeon sit down with his legs stretched out in front of him if he is strong enough, and have an assistant stand behind him to hold his waist and lean him slightly towards the back. Then you sit on his thighs.
And bind your back to his face, place his leg between your legs and secure his knee with both hands and interlock them between your fingers on his knee. Then grasp the sides of his knee strongly with your hands and have another assistant hold his other leg until the knee returns to its proper position.
And a sign that it has returned to its proper position is when you bind the leg to the thigh in a relaxed manner, without resistance. Then tighten them and secure the leg with the thigh and tie both of them together with a bandage for three or four days then release them. And he does not return to work until he has walked lightly for some days until his strength returns.
If it is difficult for you to return it to its place as we have described and it cannot be done otherwise, then use the strong pressure with the bands which I have mentioned in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
The Thirty-Third Treatise.
In the treatment of a broken ankle.
The ankle can become detached by a slight dislocation, and it may be entirely separated; its separation being inwards or outwards. Its indication is that the ankle becomes swollen to one side to which it has been separated.
As for the treatment of its dislocation, it is easy to return it and that is by being grasped with a gentle force with the hands and straightened until it returns. As for treating its separation if it has been completely separated, it should be that the healer sits cross-legged and a servant holds him strongly from behind his back in the middle of his back.
Then grasp your right hand at the top of his foot and your left hand from below it in place of the heel, then pull the foot to you with your right hand then with your left twice then extend it with your left and push the front of the foot with your right towards the leg without force.
You do that twice as we described, then push the front of the foot towards the leg in the third time while pulling with the heel, if it returns to its normal state within a session or two and you see the foot straightened, otherwise repeat the treatment on it because it will return.
If it refuses to return as we described, otherwise lay the patient on his back on the ground and place a pole or stick firmly in the ground so that it falls between his thighs, then have the servant tie his feet.
Then another servant will stretch the man either with his hands or by a rope which he ties around the man's neck, then both servants will extend themselves in opposition to each other and the band will be upright between the patient's thighs holding him so that his body does not slip down when they are extending, then the doctor will straighten the separation with his hands.
And another servant will hold the healthy leg down, and the separation will return quickly. If the separation returns and you find it to be in its proper place, then take the padding and the bandage and bind it tightly and tie the foot with the bandage downwards. It is advisable that you beware of the nerve that is behind the ankle from behind.
Lest the ligature be too tight and harm it, then leave it for two or three days; if the ligature relaxes, tighten it again and release it on the third or fourth day. The patient shall abstain from walking forty days.
If he walks before this period, do not be assured that the bone will not fracture and become corrupted, nor accept treatment thereafter. If an inflamed swelling occurs, it is necessary to apply what we have described elsewhere in treating and soothing swellings until it subsides.
The 34th Chapter.
In the treatment of a fractured finger.
It is to be straightened with a slight stretch without force, which is not difficult but easy. If the fracture is in some of the bones of the sole, it is necessary that the patient place his foot on a flat surface of ground.
Then rise and place your foot on what has come out of those joints, then step on it with your foot with great force until it returns and you see that it is straightened and no swelling appears in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot, taking up all of it so that there are two ends for you; then bind it tightly and firmly for three days. Then release it and keep him from walking for many days until it strengthens and you assure its return, if God wills.
The 35th Chapter.
In the types of fracture that occur with a wound or with a break or with both together.
When something like that occurs and his treatment and plastering fail, death often follows.
Therefore, it is not fitting for someone to attempt treatment of that sort unless he is skilled in his craft, a long-traveled and gentle soul, calm and deliberate, without rashness or haste; and in the beginning, he should only use medications that soothe hot swellings, and entrust the patient.
May God grant him safety from damage except what I have hoped for, the alleviation of his illness with a slight sickness and that you will show mercy to him in it. So return him to your time first before a hot swelling occurs.
If the part returns to what you desired, then use the treatment that quiets the hot swellings and treat the wound with whatever ointments are suitable for it. And if the jaw is broken and there has occurred a scattering of splinters in the bone, then extract them. And do as we mentioned regarding simple illnesses, what precedes in its places.
Exert your efforts and cleanse yourself from entering the path of mischief as I have preceded and advised you, for that is more lasting for your reputation and safer for your face if God wills.
